# Skill instructions: post-human-normalize

---
name: post-human-normalize
description: Validates and normalizes any file that has been modified by a human reviewer before it becomes input for the next stage. Ensures that human edits have not broken the structural contracts that downstream stages depend on. Runs after every [HUMAN] review checkpoint.
---

# post-human-normalize

## For the human

Every time you review and edit a file — approving factcheck findings, fixing claims, resolving audit issues — there is a risk that your edits break something the next stage depends on. You might merge two claims into one paragraph, accidentally delete a marker, change a citation format, or remove a field from a YAML file. These are easy mistakes to make, and the next AI stage will silently produce wrong output rather than catching the problem.

This step is a quick automated check that catches these problems before they propagate. It runs after every human review point and validates that the edited file still meets the structural requirements for the next stage.

This step does not change the meaning of anything you wrote. It only checks structure, reformats where necessary, and flags violations that need your attention before proceeding.

## When to run

After every `[HUMAN]` checkpoint in the stage order:
- After inventory-audit human review → validates `inventory.approved.yaml`
- After intro-chapter factcheck human review → validates `chapters/00-introduction.adoc`
- After claims-factcheck human review → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.claims.approved.adoc`
- After narrative-fidelity human review (if REVISE) → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.adoc`
- After comparative-chapter factcheck human review → validates `comparative.adoc`
- After character-appendix factcheck human review → validates `character-appendix.adoc`

## Checks performed

### For YAML files (`inventory.approved.yaml`, factcheck outputs)
1. All required fields present per the schema defined in the producing skill.
2. No empty required fields.
3. All `source_id` values resolve to entries in `sources.yaml` registry.
4. No YAML syntax errors.

### For claims documents (`*.claims.approved.adoc`)
1. One claim per paragraph — no merged or bundled claims.
2. Every claim paragraph has a footnote citation.
3. Every evidence token (`// EVIDENCE: ...`) is syntactically valid and references a `source_id` in `sources.yaml`.
4. All markers (`[INFERENCE:]`, `[LACUNA:]`, `[RECONSTRUCTION:]`, `[VARIANT:]`) have all required sub-fields.
5. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.

### For prose documents (intro, comparative, character-appendix, narrative chapters)
1. All markers syntactically valid with required sub-fields.
2. All evidence tokens syntactically valid.
3. Canonical chapter anchors present and unchanged.
4. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.
5. No `never_use` glossary terms present.

## Output

The validated file, with a provenance comment block appended:
```
// Validated by post-human-normalize
// Timestamp: <UTC>
// Checks passed: <list>
// Warnings: <any non-blocking issues>
```

If any check fails, the file is returned to the human with a specific error report. The pipeline does not proceed until all checks pass.

## Completion protocol

As your very last action — after all output files are written and all self-checks pass — write a completion record. This allows the pipeline runner to verify that no stage was truncated by a timeout, rate limit, or context overflow.

**File**: `books/<book>/completions/<NN>-<stage-name>.done.yaml`

Create the `completions/` directory if it does not exist.

**Format**:
```yaml
stage: "<stage-name>"
timestamp: "<UTC ISO 8601>"
status: "completed"
agent_model: "<your model name>"
outputs:
  - file: "<relative path from book dir>"
    lines: <line count>
  # repeat for each output file
summary: "<one-line description of what was produced>"
```

**Rules**:
1. Write this file only after ALL outputs are complete and verified.
2. The `lines` count must be the actual line count of each file at the time of writing — do not estimate.
3. If you were unable to complete all outputs, write the file with `status: "partial"` and list which outputs are missing in a `missing` field.
4. Never write `status: "completed"` if any output file is missing or truncated.

## Handoff
The validated file proceeds to the next stage as defined in the stage order.

---

# Input file: chapters/27-mapuche-kai-tren.claims.adoc

```
[[ch-27-mapuche-kai-tren-claims]]
== Kai-Kai vs. Tren-Tren (Mapuche) — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Related-tier (short/family) chapter on the Mapuche cosmogonic narrative
// in which the marine serpent Kai-Kai raises the sea to drown the earth and
// the mountain serpent Tren-Tren raises the mountains in response, generating
// the elevated southern-cone landscape inhabited by the Mapuche. Per brief
// 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml the chapter is "related (short/family)" with a
// target of approximately 2,500 narrative words. Variant handling is
// co-equal: regional Mapuche variations across Nguluche, Huilliche, and
// Pehuenche groups, with the chapter presenting the common narrative core
// while acknowledging regional variation. Koessler-Ilg 1962 (Cuentan los
// araucanos) supplies the principal Spanish-language narrative anchor;
// Foerster 1993, Bacigalupo 2007, Course 2011, and Faron 1964 are the
// chapter's area-specialist secondary anchors for the cosmographic /
// ritual / appropriation-framing material. The chapter opens with the
// Mapuche tradition's own framing — the Wenu Mapu / Naq Mapu / Minche
// Mapu vertical cosmography and the ngillatun ritual context — before
// invoking the comparative chaoskampf category. When it does, it
// explicitly registers that Mapuche-studies area specialists (Foerster,
// Bacigalupo, Course, Faron; Latcham as precursor) read Kai-Kai / Tren-
// Tren as cosmogonic-cataclysm and ngillatun-ritual-foundation rather
// than as cosmos-versus-chaos combat. The chapter contributes to Part II
// C7 (the inversion / transformation problem) and C8 (the refusal and
// the appropriation problem) as a related-tier non-Eurasian reframing
// of the chaoskampf category, with the area-specialist objection
// characterised per the brief as comparable in strength to the Ch26
// Pariacaca case and milder than the Ch32 Nanabozho refusal.

=== Source overview

This chapter treats the Mapuche cosmogonic narrative in which two serpent-beings — Kai-Kai, who lives in the sea, and Tren-Tren, who lives on the mountain — raise water and earth against one another, with the resulting flood-and-mountain rivalry generating the elevated southern-cone landscape that the Mapuche inhabit and saving the ancestors of the Mapuche people from extinction in the sea.footnote:[Per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml summary on the marine serpent Kai-Kai raising the sea to drown the earth, on the mountain serpent Tren-Tren raising the mountains in response to save the Mapuche, and on the two-serpent cosmogony preserving a flood-and-height memory; Koessler-Ilg 1962, *Cuentan los araucanos: Mitos, leyendas y tradiciones* (Buenos Aires: Editorial Mar Océano, 1962; OCLC 4124869), sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony as the principal Spanish-language narrative anchor for the cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The narrative survives in oral tradition rather than in any pre-conquest written record: the Mapuche of present-day south-central Chile and southern-cone Argentina did not develop an alphabetic writing system before contact with Spanish-speaking colonisers, and the principal record of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle is the body of nineteenth- and twentieth-century ethnographic compilations recorded from Mapudungun-speaking informants by Spanish- and German-speaking ethnographers and folklorists working in Chile and Argentina from approximately the 1860s onward.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, introductory matter on the oral-tradition character of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle and on the body of Mapuche oral narrative she records from Mapudungun-speaking informants in the 1920s-30s; Foerster 1993, *Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche* (Santiago: Editorial Universitaria, 1993; ISBN 9789561109315), introductory matter on the textual situation of Mapuche cosmogonic narrative and on the body of nineteenth- and twentieth-century ethnographic compilations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

The chapter's principal narrative anchor is Bertha Koessler-Ilg's *Cuentan los araucanos: Mitos, leyendas y tradiciones* (Buenos Aires: Editorial Mar Océano, 1962), a Spanish-language compilation expanding her earlier German-language *Märchenerzähler ohne Buch* of 1928, built from oral narratives recorded in the 1920s and 1930s among Mapudungun-speaking communities of the Argentine Andes; the chapter's area-specialist secondary anchors are Rolf Foerster González's *Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche* (1993) on the Wenu Mapu / Naq Mapu / Minche Mapu vertical cosmography and the *ngillatun* communal ritual, Ana Mariella Bacigalupo's *Shamans of the Foye Tree* (2007) on contemporary *machi* practice, Magnus Course's *Becoming Mapuche* (2011) on Mapuche personhood and ritual, and Louis C. Faron's *Hawks of the Sun* (1964) as the mid-twentieth-century classic English-language structural-ritual anchor on which the later three build.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg, B., *Cuentan los araucanos: Mitos, leyendas y tradiciones* (Buenos Aires: Editorial Mar Océano, 1962; OCLC 4124869), as the chapter's principal Spanish-language narrative anchor; Foerster González, R., *Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche* (Santiago: Editorial Universitaria, 1993; ISBN 9789561109315); Bacigalupo, A.M., *Shamans of the Foye Tree: Gender, Power, and Healing among Chilean Mapuche* (Austin: University of Texas Press, 2007; ISBN 9780292716599); Course, M., *Becoming Mapuche: Person and Ritual in Indigenous Chile* (Urbana: University of Illinois Press, 2011; ISBN 9780252078163); Faron, L.C., *Hawks of the Sun: Mapuche Morality and Its Ritual Attributes* (Pittsburgh: University of Pittsburgh Press, 1964; OCLC 247070); per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml sources entries.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The earliest written witnesses to Mapuche cosmogonic narrative are colonial-period Spanish accounts compiled in the seventeenth century by Spanish missionaries and soldiers who lived among the Mapuche during and after the long Arauco War — most notably the Jesuit Diego de Rosales's *Historia general del reino de Chile, Flandes Indiano* (composed c. 1670) and Francisco Núñez de Pineda y Bascuñán's *Cautiverio feliz* (composed c. 1673) — with the modern ethnographic record assembled in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries by figures including Rodolfo Lenz, Ricardo E. Latcham (whose 1924 *La organización social y las creencias religiosas de los antiguos araucanos* was the first systematic ethnological synthesis), and Félix José de Augusta with Sigifredo Schindler de Moesbach (whose 1934-36 Mapudungun-language collections remain a standard reference); these earlier sources are named in body prose per the named-but-flagged pattern (Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 precedent) since they are not currently pinned in sources.yaml.footnote:[Foerster 1993 on the colonial-period Spanish witnesses to Mapuche cosmogonic narrative (Rosales SJ c. 1670; Núñez de Pineda c. 1673) and on the late-nineteenth- and early-twentieth-century ethnographic compilation work of Lenz, Latcham 1924, and Augusta-Moesbach 1934-36; per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml source_anchoring_notes on the named-but-flagged disposition of these earlier sources.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

[LACUNA: pre-conquest written-prose attestations of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle are not available, since pre-contact Mapuche record-keeping was oral rather than alphabetic, and the surviving textual witnesses are all post-conquest compositions filtered through Spanish-language transcription. The colonial-period Spanish witnesses (Diego de Rosales SJ's *Historia general del reino de Chile*, c. 1670; Francisco Núñez de Pineda y Bascuñán's *Cautiverio feliz*, c. 1673) and the nineteenth- and twentieth-century ethnographic compilations (Lenz; Latcham 1924; Augusta-Moesbach 1934-36; Koessler-Ilg 1962) all post-date Spanish contact and were composed in Spanish or German rather than in Mapudungun. Pre-contact ritual contexts (the *ngillatun* communal ritual; *machi* shamanic practice; oral *epew* and *nütram* narrative-and-history performance) are partially recoverable through these post-conquest textual sources, through twentieth- and twenty-first-century ethnographic fieldwork in still-active Mapuche communities, and through archaeological evidence at ritual sites in south-central Chile and southern-cone Argentina. | source: brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml on the post-conquest filtering caveat applicable to the Mapuche material; Koessler-Ilg 1962 introductory matter on the oral-tradition character of the cycle; Foerster 1993 on the textual situation of Mapuche cosmogonic narrative and on the colonial-period and ethnographic compilation history (Rosales SJ, Núñez de Pineda, Lenz, Latcham, Augusta-Moesbach). | scholarly_reconstruction: yes — the chapter's narrative reconstruction proceeds within the body of Mapuche oral-and-ethnographic record itself, taking Koessler-Ilg 1962 as the principal narrative anchor and Foerster 1993, Bacigalupo 2007, Course 2011, and Faron 1964 as the area-specialist secondary anchors. Cross-tradition reconstruction is excluded per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" point 1; the modern ethnographic record (still-active *ngillatun* ritual; ongoing *machi* practice; oral narrative still in performance) supplies a continuing-tradition witness that distinguishes the Mapuche situation from the more strictly archival situations of the Eurasian chapters in the present book.]

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth typology is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton University Press, 1987), whose Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey supplies the typological framing within which the present chapter situates the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle as a non-cognate parallel to (rather than a member of) Forsyth's Near-Eastern-and-Judeo-Christian arc; Forsyth supplies the typological framing only — not Mapuche-content coverage, which Forsyth does not undertake — and the area-specialist objection to the chaoskampf framing of the Mapuche material is registered separately in this chapter's [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic); Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Mapuche material, and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Mapuche-content scholarly coverage; per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml sources.secondary entry on the not-for-Mapuche-coverage disclaimer.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

=== The Mapuche cosmographic frame: Wenu Mapu, Naq Mapu, Minche Mapu

The narrative's geographical and cultural setting is the Mapuche homeland of present-day south-central Chile (between the Bío-Bío and Toltén rivers, with adjacent areas to the north and south) and southern-cone Argentina (the Andean foothills of Neuquén, Río Negro, and Chubut), a temperate-rainforest landscape of forested mountain slopes, intermontane valleys, lakes, and a long Pacific coastline, with the cycle rooted in specific named landscape features within this geography that figure in the narrative as the bodies and the sites of the two named serpent-beings.footnote:[Foerster 1993, introductory matter on the Mapuche homeland of south-central Chile and southern-cone Argentina, on the temperate-rainforest landscape of forested mountain slopes, intermontane valleys, lakes, and a long Pacific coastline, and on the rooting of Mapuche cosmogonic narrative in specific named landscape features that figure as the bodies and the sites of the named serpent-beings; Koessler-Ilg 1962 on the regional geography of the cycle's narrative settings.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

The cycle's principal cosmographic framework is the Mapuche vertical-cosmographic system in which the world is layered into the *Wenu Mapu* (the upper land, sky, and the dwelling of *ngen* and beneficent powers), the *Naq Mapu* (the middle land, the surface of the earth where humans live), and the *Minche Mapu* (the under-land, the realm of the *wekufü* and other adversarial powers), with Kai-Kai and Tren-Tren operating across the boundary between *Naq Mapu* and the surrounding water-and-mountain registers rather than as figures of a primordial chaos preceding cosmos.footnote:[Foerster 1993, on the Mapuche vertical-cosmographic system of *Wenu Mapu* (upper land), *Naq Mapu* (middle land, the surface where humans live), and *Minche Mapu* (under-land), on the *ngen* (place-spirits / owners) of the upper land and the *wekufü* (adversarial powers) of the under-land; Course 2011, on the cosmological framework underpinning Mapuche ritual practice and the personhood-cosmography integration; per glossary 'Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren' first_mention_gloss on the paired Mapuche marine and mountain serpents.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

Kai-Kai (also rendered Kaikai-filu in some twentieth-century compilations, with *filu* the Mapudungun word for serpent) is in the cycle the marine serpent of the deep ocean, associated with the destructive power of the sea — tidal flooding, cataclysmic wave-action, the rising of waters that threatens to overwhelm the land — and is presented in the narrative not as an unmotivated primordial chaos but as a named serpent-being acting within an already-existing Mapuche cosmographic order, with Kai-Kai's flood-action being the cycle's principal cosmogonic-cataclysm rather than a chaos-versus-cosmos opening.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on Kai-Kai (also Kaikai-filu) as the marine serpent of the deep ocean, on the destructive power of the sea associated with the figure (tidal flooding, cataclysmic wave-action, the rising of waters), and on Kai-Kai as a named serpent-being within an already-existing Mapuche cosmographic order rather than as an unmotivated primordial chaos; Foerster 1993 on the cosmogonic-cataclysm reading of the Kai-Kai flood-action.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

Tren-Tren (also rendered Trentren-filu in some compilations) is in the cycle the mountain serpent of the high land, associated with the protective and uplifting power of the earth — the rising of mountains, the sheltering of the Mapuche on high ground, the provision of refuge from the rising sea — and is presented as the protective counterpart to Kai-Kai rather than as a hero who slays Kai-Kai, with the resolution of the cycle being a mutual-rising stalemate rather than a kill-and-eradicate combat-outcome.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on Tren-Tren (also Trentren-filu) as the mountain serpent of the high land, on the protective and uplifting power of the earth associated with the figure (the rising of mountains, the sheltering of the Mapuche on high ground), and on Tren-Tren's role as the protective counterpart to Kai-Kai rather than as a hero who slays Kai-Kai; Foerster 1993 on the mutual-rising stalemate as the cycle's resolution rather than a kill-and-eradicate combat-outcome.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

=== The flood: Kai-Kai raises the sea

The cycle's narrative crisis begins with Kai-Kai's raising of the sea: the marine serpent rises from the ocean depths and causes the waters to swell, with tidal floods overwhelming the coastal lowlands and rising progressively up the river valleys and onto the inland plains, threatening to drown all of the Naq Mapu and to extinguish the human and animal populations of the Mapuche homeland.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on the cycle's narrative opening with Kai-Kai's raising of the sea, on the marine serpent rising from the ocean depths and causing the waters to swell, on tidal floods overwhelming the coastal lowlands and rising progressively up river valleys and onto inland plains, and on the threat to drown all of the *Naq Mapu* and extinguish the human and animal populations of the Mapuche homeland.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The flood's narrative function within the cycle is cosmogonic-cataclysm rather than cosmogonic-establishment-from-nothing: the Mapuche world is already populated and ordered before Kai-Kai's flood begins, and the flood is a near-extinction crisis within an existing cosmos rather than the cosmos's first ordering, with the cycle's load-bearing narrative work being the preservation and reconfiguration of an already-existing world rather than the establishment of cosmos out of chaos.footnote:[Foerster 1993, on the cosmogonic-cataclysm character of the Kai-Kai flood as a near-extinction crisis within an already-existing Mapuche cosmos rather than as cosmogonic establishment from nothing, and on the cycle's load-bearing narrative work as the preservation and reconfiguration of an already-existing world; Course 2011 on the cosmological framework underpinning the cycle's interpretation as cataclysm-within-cosmos rather than cosmos-from-chaos.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle's principal narrative work as cosmogonic-cataclysm-within-existing-cosmos (the flood-and-height crisis within an already-populated Mapuche world, with the cycle's outcome being landscape-reconfiguration and ritual-foundation rather than cosmos-establishment-ex-novo) rather than as cosmogonic combat in the strong cosmos-versus-chaos sense is a synthesis of the brief's framing per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml summary, of Foerster 1993's cosmographic framework, of Course 2011 and Bacigalupo 2007's contemporary anthropological readings, and of Faron 1964's mid-twentieth-century structural-ritual reading — treated together as the chapter's load-bearing reading. | basis: brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml explicitly classifies the cycle's pattern as a two-serpent cosmogony that "does not map cleanly onto chaoskampf"; Foerster 1993 develops the cosmogonic-cataclysm reading via the *Wenu* / *Naq* / *Minche Mapu* framework; Bacigalupo 2007, Course 2011, and Faron 1964 converge on the *ngillatun*-ritual-foundation reading. | risk: alternative comparativist readings are available and have historically been advanced (older twentieth-century structuralist treatments tended to assimilate the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren material to a cosmos-versus-chaos pattern, and Latcham 1924's foundational synthesis is sometimes read in that comparativist register); the chapter's cosmogonic-cataclysm-within-existing-cosmos reading is the area-specialist consensus per the brief but is not the only available scholarly position, and the chapter holds it as the load-bearing reading on the strength of the area-specialist citation policy at scope.md §"Appropriation protocol".]

=== The mountain rising: Tren-Tren raises the land

The cycle's narrative resolution begins with Tren-Tren's response: as Kai-Kai's waters rise from the sea, the mountain serpent rises from beneath the land and causes the mountains to lift, with high ground rising progressively higher to outpace the rising waters and the Mapuche people fleeing upslope onto the rising peaks where Tren-Tren's body provides shelter and refuge from the sea.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on Tren-Tren's response with the mountain serpent rising from beneath the land, on the mountains lifting progressively higher to outpace the rising waters, on the Mapuche people fleeing upslope onto the rising peaks, and on Tren-Tren's body providing shelter and refuge from the sea; Faron 1964 on the protective-mountain-serpent figure as a structural feature of the Mapuche cosmogonic narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The two serpents' opposing motions — Kai-Kai pushing the waters up, Tren-Tren pushing the mountains up — produce the cycle's principal combat-image: a vertical race between sea and mountain in which neither serpent kills or is killed, with the flood-and-height engagement being a sustained mutual-rising rather than a single decisive blow, and with the Mapuche on the rising peaks being the narrative's centre of attention as the cycle's beneficiary-survivors of a combat between two non-human powers.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on the two serpents' opposing motions as the cycle's principal combat-image, on the vertical race between sea and mountain as a sustained mutual-rising rather than a single decisive blow, and on the Mapuche on the rising peaks as the cycle's beneficiary-survivors of a combat between two non-human powers; Bacigalupo 2007 on the two-serpent structural pattern as distinctive to Mapuche cosmogonic material relative to the hero-versus-monster pattern of much Eurasian combat-narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The cycle's outcome is not the killing of Kai-Kai by Tren-Tren or the killing of Tren-Tren by Kai-Kai but a stable equilibrium: the waters subside, the mountains remain elevated, and the Mapuche descend from the high peaks onto the now-permanently-elevated and rugged landscape that the rising-mountain action has produced — a landscape-aetiological resolution in which the southern-cone Andean topography is itself the cycle's load-bearing trace and the Mapuche territorial occupation of that elevated terrain is the cycle's narrative point.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on the cycle's outcome as stable equilibrium rather than killing — waters subside, mountains remain elevated, the Mapuche descend onto the permanently-elevated and rugged landscape — and on the southern-cone Andean topography as the cycle's load-bearing landscape-aetiological trace; Foerster 1993 on the landscape-aetiological resolution and on the Mapuche territorial occupation of the elevated terrain as the cycle's narrative point.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

=== Regional variants: Nguluche, Huilliche, Pehuenche

The cycle is attested across the principal regional Mapuche groupings — Nguluche of the central Mapuche heartland between the Bío-Bío and the Toltén; Huilliche of the southern Mapuche territories south of the Toltén into the Chiloé archipelago; and Pehuenche of the Andean cordillera and adjacent eastern slopes — with the common narrative core (Kai-Kai's marine flood; Tren-Tren's mountain rising; the Mapuche refuge on the rising peaks; the landscape-aetiological resolution) shared across the regional variants, and with regional-specific narrative elements (specific named mountain refuges; particular animal companions of the human survivors; details of Kai-Kai's defeat or pacification) varying among the groupings.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962, sections on the cycle's attestation across the principal Mapuche regional groupings — Nguluche of the central Mapuche heartland, Huilliche of the southern territories and Chiloé, Pehuenche of the Andean cordillera and eastern slopes — and on the shared common narrative core (Kai-Kai's marine flood, Tren-Tren's mountain rising, the Mapuche refuge on the rising peaks, the landscape-aetiological resolution); per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml variants entry on the co-equal regional variation across Nguluche, Huilliche, and Pehuenche.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

The regional-specific variants exhibit characteristic localised performance-anchoring rather than disagreement about overall narrative structure: Huilliche material identifies the named refuge-mountain with specific peaks of the southern Andean cordillera and integrates the cycle with further Huilliche-specific cosmogonic-and-aetiological material on the local landscape, while Pehuenche material (recorded on both Argentine and Chilean sides of the cordillera) integrates the cycle with the Pehuenche cultural-and-ritual association with the *pehuén* (Araucaria araucana / monkey-puzzle tree), whose seeds are the staple food of the Pehuenche economy, with some recordings tying the post-flood landscape's establishment to the founding of the *pehuén* groves on the rising mountains.footnote:[Koessler-Ilg 1962 on Huilliche-specific variants identifying the refuge-mountain with specific peaks of the southern cordillera, and on Pehuenche-specific recordings tying the post-flood landscape's establishment to the founding of *pehuén* (Araucaria araucana) groves on the rising mountains; Foerster 1993 on the regional-specific identifications as localised performance-anchoring; Course 2011 on the Pehuenche subsistence base and the integration of cosmogonic narrative with subsistence-aetiological material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=koessler-ilg-cuentan ; loc=Cuentan los araucanos, sections on Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren and the flood-and-height cosmogony

=== Ngillatun and the cycle's ritual context

The Mapuche cosmogonic material does not function in isolation from ritual but is embedded in the *ngillatun* communal ritual — a multi-day public ceremonial gathering held at named ritual sites (the *nguillatuwe*) on a calendrical schedule, presided over by the *machi* (the Mapuche shaman, traditionally though not exclusively a woman) and incorporating prayers, sacrificial offerings, dances, and ritualised speech directed to the *ngen* of the upper land — within which the cosmogonic narratives are performed, transmitted, and re-actualised across generations.footnote:[Foerster 1993, on the *ngillatun* communal ritual as a multi-day public ceremonial gathering held at named ritual sites (*nguillatuwe*) on a calendrical schedule, on its presidency by the *machi* (the Mapuche shaman), on its incorporation of prayers, sacrificial offerings, dances, and ritualised speech directed to the *ngen* of the upper land, and on the embedding of Mapuche cosmogonic narratives in the *ngillatun* ritual context for performance and transmission across generations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

The Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle's ritual function within *ngillatun* and within the wider Mapuche ritual life is the foundational-charter function: the cycle establishes the legitimacy of Mapuche occupation of the elevated southern-cone landscape (Tren-Tren's rising mountains as the place where the Mapuche took refuge and from which they descended to populate the present-day terrain), the legitimacy of the *ngillatun* ritual itself (as the continuing communal address to the powers whose combat established the Mapuche cosmographic situation), and the ritual register within which the *machi* operates as an intermediary between *Naq Mapu* and the powers above and below.footnote:[Bacigalupo 2007, on the foundational-charter function of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle within Mapuche ritual life — establishing the legitimacy of Mapuche occupation of the elevated southern-cone landscape, the legitimacy of the *ngillatun* ritual, and the ritual register of the *machi* as intermediary between *Naq Mapu* and the powers above and below; Course 2011 on the foundational-charter function of cosmogonic narrative in Mapuche ritual life and on the integration of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle into the contemporary *ngillatun* practice.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=bacigalupo-shamans-foye ; loc=Shamans of the Foye Tree, on machi and contemporary Mapuche ritual

The cycle's continuing ritual function in still-active Mapuche communities of Chile and Argentina is significant for the chapter's status: where most of the Eurasian combat-myth material treated in the present book survives only in the archival record of an antique tradition no longer in continuous performance, the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle remains in active oral and ritual transmission within communities that perform *ngillatun* on a continuing basis and within which contemporary *machi* maintain the lineage of ritual practice, with the modern ethnographic record (Bacigalupo 2007; Course 2011) being a record of a still-living tradition rather than of an archival reconstruction.footnote:[Bacigalupo 2007, on the continuing ritual function of Mapuche cosmogonic narrative in still-active Mapuche communities of Chile and Argentina, on the contemporary practice of *ngillatun* and the contemporary *machi* lineage; Course 2011 on the still-living-tradition character of the Mapuche ethnographic record relative to the archival situation of most Eurasian combat-myth material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=course-becoming-mapuche ; loc=Becoming Mapuche, on Mapuche personhood and the cosmological framework of ritual

=== Typology, area-specialist framing, and forward-links

The Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle's place within the present book's chaoskampf typology is — when taken in the area-specialist reading — a related-tier (rather than core-tier) case in which a combat-pattern is structurally present (two named serpent-beings opposing one another with sustained vertical action) but the cosmogonic-establishment-ex-novo and order-versus-chaos load-bearing functions of Eurasian chaoskampf are not load-bearing for the Mapuche material, with the cycle's principal narrative work being cosmogonic-cataclysm-within-existing-cosmos and *ngillatun*-ritual-foundation rather than cosmos-versus-chaos combat.footnote:[Per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml summary on the cycle's two-serpent structure not mapping cleanly onto chaoskampf and on the related-tier classification; scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the cosmogonic-combat work is weaker; Foerster 1993, Bacigalupo 2007, Course 2011, Faron 1964 on the area-specialist consensus reading of the cycle as cosmogonic-cataclysm-and-ngillatun-ritual-foundation rather than as cosmos-versus-chaos combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: Mapuche-studies area specialists (Rolf Foerster González in *Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche*, 1993; Ana Mariella Bacigalupo in *Shamans of the Foye Tree*, 2007; Magnus Course in *Becoming Mapuche*, 2011; Louis C. Faron in *Hawks of the Sun*, 1964; with Ricardo E. Latcham 1924's *La organización social y las creencias religiosas de los antiguos araucanos* as the foundational early-twentieth-century precursor named in body prose without a sources.yaml entry per the named-but-flagged pattern of Ch23 FC-02 and Ch26 FC-02) read the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle as cosmogonic-cataclysm-within-existing-cosmos and ngillatun-ritual-foundation rather than as chaoskampf in the strong cosmos-versus-chaos sense. The substance of the area-specialist objection is that the cycle's two-serpent structure (with no hero-figure and no kill-and-eradicate outcome, and with a non-divine human population as the narrative's beneficiary-centre) and the cycle's cataclysm-within-already-existing-cosmos framing (with the Mapuche world already populated and ordered before Kai-Kai's flood begins) do not match the cosmos-versus-chaos opposition that the Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf-typology presupposes; the cycle's narrative work is the foundational-charter establishment of the *ngillatun* ritual life and the legitimisation of Mapuche territorial occupation of the elevated southern-cone landscape rather than the cosmogonic establishment of cosmic order from primordial chaos. The area-specialist objection-strength is comparable to the Ch26 Pariacaca case (where Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, and Dean read the cycle as wak'a-succession rather than as chaos-versus-order combat) — both are mid-spectrum objections in which the area-specialist reading does accommodate some combat-myth framing while objecting to the chaos-order framing — and milder than the Ch32 Nanabozho case (where Hallowell, Johnston, T. Smith, and Vizenor reject combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing entirely in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing). Part II C7 (inversion / transformation; ch. 40 of the present volume) and Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation; ch. 41) are the loci where the comparative analysis must address this objection rather than paper over it; the present chapter functions as a worked instance of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement at scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3, and forwards the objection to C7 and C8 for the comparative-engagement work that scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 2 requires of the comparative chapters.]

The cycle's distinctive contribution to the present book's wider comparative argument is the two-serpent-with-human-beneficiary-centre pattern: where Eurasian chaoskampf-tradition narratives typically oppose a single sovereign or champion deity to a single serpentine adversary and place the deity's victory at the narrative's centre, the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle opposes two serpent-beings to one another and places the survival of a non-divine human population on the rising mountains at the narrative's centre — a structural pattern that the methods sidebar (chapter 33) and Part II C7-C8 will engage with as one of the present book's load-bearing examples of the spectrum of non-Eurasian combat-narrative architectures.footnote:[Per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml summary on the two-serpent-with-human-beneficiary-centre pattern as distinctive to the Mapuche material; Faron 1964 on the structural-ritual framework within which the human-beneficiary-centre operates; Course 2011 on the contemporary anthropological reading of the human-beneficiary-centre as integrated with Mapuche personhood and ritual.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faron-hawks-of-sun ; loc=Hawks of the Sun, on the structural-ritual framework

The chapter's principal forward-link is to Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem, ch. 41) — alongside the Ch26 Pariacaca contribution as a working example of the *milder appropriation* sub-problem, in which the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing but accommodates combat-myth framing — with a secondary forward-link to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, ch. 40) as a *transformation* sub-problem instance, and a methods-sidebar contribution (Ch. 33) on three methodological points: the area-specialist citation policy (Foerster, Bacigalupo, Course, Faron with Latcham named-but-flagged per Ch23/Ch26 precedent), the post-conquest filtering issue, and the still-living-tradition methodological situation (which distinguishes the Mapuche material from most of the Eurasian combat-myth material in the book).footnote:[Per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C7 and C8, on the milder-than-Nanabozho characterisation, and on the area-specialist citation policy; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 2 on the comparative chapter's task of addressing area-specialist objections; chapter 33 methods sidebar (per toc.yaml) as the chapter that articulates these methodological points at full length; Bacigalupo 2007 and Course 2011 on the still-living-tradition methodological situation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=foerster-religiosidad-mapuche ; loc=Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche, on the Wenu / Naq / Minche Mapu cosmographic system and ngillatun ritual

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to chapter 26 on Pariacaca and the amaru, with which the present chapter shares the southern-cone (broadly Andean) geographic placement within the Americas cluster, the post-conquest textual-record situation, and the area-specialist objection to the chaos-order framing; the two chapters are paired in the Americas cluster (per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 10) as related-tier non-Eurasian reframings exhibiting mid-strength area-specialist objections, with the Mapuche chapter adding a still-living-tradition methodological dimension that the more strictly archival Pariacaca chapter does not foreground.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 10 (Americas) on the chapter ordering of 24 (Huitzilopochtli core-tier), 25 (Mesoamerican family related-tier), 26 (Pariacaca related-tier), 27 (Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren related-family); per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml and brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml on the related-tier classifications; Bacigalupo 2007 and Course 2011 on the still-living-tradition methodological dimension distinctive to the Mapuche material relative to the more archival situation of the Pariacaca cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=course-becoming-mapuche ; loc=Becoming Mapuche, on Mapuche personhood and the cosmological framework of ritual

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Andean (Pariacaca and the amaru, Ch. 26) — preceding Americas-cluster Andean related-tier chapter; structural and framing-stance parallel between the wak'a-succession reading of the Pariacaca cycle (area-specialist consensus per Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, Dean) and the cosmogonic-cataclysm-and-ngillatun-ritual-foundation reading of the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle (area-specialist consensus per Foerster, Bacigalupo, Course, Faron; with Latcham as precursor); both chapters exhibit mid-spectrum area-specialist objections to the chaos-order framing while accommodating some combat-myth framing, paired in the Americas cluster as two related-tier non-Eurasian reframings of the chaoskampf category for Part II C8; per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml special_instructions on the comparable objection-strength characterisation.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesoamerica (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — Americas-cluster colonial-filtering parallel: where Ch24 handles the post-conquest Spanish-friar redaction of the Mexica narrative material (Sahagún, Durán, Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas) via the [LACUNA] marker on pre-conquest written-prose absence and continuous methodological registration, the present chapter handles the analogous post-conquest filtering of the Mapuche material (Rosales SJ, Núñez de Pineda, Lenz, Latcham, Augusta-Moesbach, Koessler-Ilg) via the same structural pattern, with the Mapuche material adding a still-living-tradition methodological dimension that distinguishes it from the more strictly archival Mexica situation; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the post-conquest source-record-filtering issue.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — combat-as-cosmogony comparative pattern: where Marduk's victory over Tiamat establishes the cosmos through dismemberment of the defeated adversary's body, the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle's mutual-rising stalemate establishes the southern-cone landscape through sustained cosmographic reorganisation rather than through dismemberment-cosmogony; the structural contrast between dismemberment-from-defeated-adversary cosmogony (Marduk-Tiamat) and mutual-rising-from-two-serpents landscape-aetiology (Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren) is registered for Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) as a worked example of the spectrum of cosmogonic-combat architectures within the book.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Northeast Asia / China (Gonggong, Ch. 21) — water-and-mountain cosmographic-reorganisation parallel: where Gonggong's striking of Mount Buzhou tilts the cosmos and reorganises the hydrography of China through a single decisive blow, the Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle reorganises the southern-cone landscape through a sustained mutual-rising of two serpents; both cycles use water-and-mountain as principal cosmographic elements and result in landscape-cosmographic reorganisation, but the Mapuche cycle's two-serpent structure with human-beneficiary-centre is structurally distinct from the Chinese material's deity-versus-cosmographic-pillar pattern.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi (Ch. 23) — co-related-tier non-Eurasian chapter where the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaoskampf framing on grounds of an alternative load-bearing function (sword-etiology and metallurgical-allegory in the Susanoo case; cosmogonic-cataclysm-and-ngillatun-ritual-foundation in the Mapuche case); the present chapter, Ch23, and Ch26 are paired as worked instances of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement, with all three opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and all three contributing to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem) and C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem) as related-tier non-Eurasian reframings.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Nanabozho / Mishipeshu (Ch. 32) — the present chapter is a milder case than the Anishinaabe refusal (and shares the cosmogonic-cataclysm-rather-than-chaoskampf framing typical of indigenous-American cosmogonic material): where the Hallowell-Johnston-Smith-Vizenor area-specialist tradition for the Anishinaabe material rejects combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing entirely in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing, the Foerster-Bacigalupo-Course-Faron area-specialist tradition for the Mapuche material accommodates some combat-myth framing while objecting to the chaos-order framing; the contrast between the two cases is itself one of the methods sidebar's (Ch. 33) load-bearing illustrations of the spectrum of area-specialist objection-strengths within the present book; per brief 27-mapuche-kai-tren.yaml special_instructions on the milder-than-Nanabozho characterisation.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the present chapter is a worked instance of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement (scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3): the chapter exhibits in concrete narrative-and-citation-discipline detail what it looks like to open with the tradition's own framing (Mapudungun oral-tradition material in still-living transmission, embedded in the *Wenu Mapu* / *Naq Mapu* / *Minche Mapu* vertical cosmography and the *ngillatun* communal-ritual context per Foerster, Bacigalupo, Course, Faron) before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and to hold the comparative category loosely where area-specialists require it; the chapter further exhibits the still-living-tradition methodological situation (continuing oral transmission and continuing *ngillatun* practice in active Mapuche communities) as a distinctive methodological feature that the methods sidebar can foreground alongside the more strictly archival situations of the Eurasian chapters.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony, Ch. 34) — the present chapter contributes a non-Eurasian comparandum for C1 in the cosmogonic-cataclysm-within-existing-cosmos register, with the cycle's two-serpent mutual-rising landscape-aetiological resolution paired against the dismemberment-from-defeated-adversary cosmogonies of Marduk-Tiamat (Ch. 3) and Ninurta-Asag (Ch. 1) and against the cosmographic-pillar-displacement cosmogony of Gonggong (Ch. 21) as worked examples of the spectrum of cosmogonic-combat architectures.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, Ch. 40) — the present chapter is a working example of the *transformation* sub-problem: a story in which the chaoskampf category is transformed when the area-specialist account (cosmogonic-cataclysm-and-ngillatun-ritual-foundation rather than cosmos-versus-chaos combat) is taken as load-bearing; this is to be distinguished from the inversion sub-problem proper (anchored by the Bida material in chapter 28, where the slaying inverts the order-establishing function), and contributes to C7 alongside the Ch23 Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi and Ch26 Pariacaca contributions as related-tier non-Eurasian reframings.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem, Ch. 41) — the present chapter is a working example of the *milder appropriation* sub-problem alongside Ch26 Pariacaca: a story in which the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing of the Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf category (but not to combat-myth framing as such), with the chapter contributing to C8 alongside the Ch23 Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi material, the Ch26 Pariacaca material, the Ch29 Mwindo / Kirimu material, the Ch32 Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material, and the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks); the spectrum of objection-strengths across these chapters is one of C8's load-bearing methodological contributions to the present book.

    triangulations_assumed: >
      (1) KAI-KAI / TREN-TREN CYCLE IN KOESSLER-ILG: The cycle's presence in Cuentan
      los araucanos (or Tradiciones araucanas — see identifier concern above) is
      well-established in the Mapuche-studies literature. The cycle is the central
      Mapuche cosmogonic narrative and is attested in all major compilations including
      Koessler-Ilg's work on Mapuche oral tradition from the Neuquén communities.
      PASS on content plausibility regardless of the edition identifier concern.
      (2) FOERSTER 1993 ON WENU / NAQ / MINCHE MAPU: The three-tier vertical
      cosmographic system (Wenu Mapu / Naq Mapu / Minche Mapu) and the ngillatun
      communal ritual are the principal subjects of Foerster 1993 per trained knowledge
      and per the AbeBooks and repository descriptions of the work. The book is
      described as covering "cosmographic representations, rituals, religious
      institutions and identity." PASS on content plausibility.
      (3) MAPUCHE HOMELAND GEOGRAPHY: South-central Chile (Bío-Bío to Toltén rivers)
      and southern-cone Argentina (Neuquén, Río Negro, Chubut) are the correct
      geographic locators for the Mapuche homeland. PASS.
      (4) ARAUCO WAR CONTEXT FOR COLONIAL WITNESSES: The identification of Rosales SJ
      and Núñez de Pineda as colonial-period Spanish witnesses working in the context
      of the Arauco War is correct. Rosales SJ composed the Historia general del reino
      de Chile c. 1670 (published posthumously 1877-78); Núñez de Pineda composed
      Cautiverio feliz c. 1673 (published 1863). Both are standard references in
      Mapuche-studies colonial-period scholarship. PASS.
      (5) KAIKAI-FILU / TRENTREN-FILU AS ALTERNATE FORMS: The filu-suffixed forms
      (Kaikai-filu, Trentren-filu) appear in twentieth-century ethnographic compilations,
      with filu being the Mapudungun word for serpent. This is confirmed per trained
      knowledge of Mapuche linguistic and ethnographic scholarship. PASS.
      (6) NGILLATUN AS MULTI-DAY COMMUNAL RITUAL WITH MACHI PRESIDENCY: The ngillatun
      as a multi-day communal fertility-and-cosmogonic ritual presided over by the machi
      (shaman) is the standard description in the Mapuche-studies literature
      (Foerster 1993, Bacigalupo 2007, Course 2011, Faron 1964). PASS.
      (7) PEHUENCHE AND PEHUÉN (ARAUCARIA ARAUCANA): The Pehuenche association with
      the pehuén (Araucaria araucana / monkey-puzzle tree) and its seeds as a subsistence
      staple is a well-established ethnographic fact in Mapuche-studies. PASS.
      (8) FORSYTH 1987 SCOPE: Forsyth's survey runs Sumer through Judeo-Christian
      apocalyptic; it does not treat Mapuche material. The single EVIDENCE token's
      disclaimer is accurate. PASS.
      (9) WEKUFÜ AS ADVERSARIAL POWER: The wekufü as adversarial powers of the Minche
      Mapu is consistent with the standard cosmographic description in Foerster 1993
      and Bacigalupo 2007. PASS.
      (10) LATCHAM 1924 AS FIRST SYSTEMATIC ETHNOLOGICAL SYNTHESIS: Ricardo E. Latcham's
      La organización social y las creencias religiosas de los antiguos araucanos (1924,
      Santiago: Imprenta Cervantes) is standardly referenced as the first systematic
      ethnological synthesis in Mapuche-studies. PASS.
      (11) AUGUSTA-MOESBACH 1934-36 MAPUDUNGUN COLLECTIONS: Félix José de Augusta with
      Sigifredo Schindler de Moesbach worked on Mapudungun linguistic-and-narrative
      collections in the 1930s; the 1934-36 dating for their principal collections is
      consistent with trained knowledge of the Capuchin mission documentation of Mapuche
      language and oral tradition. PASS.
      (12) MARKER COUNT AND STRUCTURE: The document contains exactly 1 LACUNA, 1 INFERENCE,
      and 1 APPROPRIATION-FLAG (no VARIANT, no RECONSTRUCTION), verified by direct count
      of the claims document. Per the brief's description and the task instructions,
      this is the expected marker count. PASS.

# FINDINGS

findings:

  - id: FC-01
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: identifier-mismatch
    location: >
      sources.yaml entry for foerster-religiosidad-mapuche (line 561);
      chapters/27-mapuche-kai-tren.claims.adoc, all 8 EVIDENCE tokens citing
      foerster-religiosidad-mapuche (lines 37, 43, 56, 70, 96, 107, 115, 118).
    claim_text: >
      "Foerster González, R., *Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche* (Santiago:
      Editorial Universitaria, 1993; ISBN 9789561109315)"
    problem: >
      The ISBN recorded in sources.yaml and in the chapter's footnotes
      (9789561109315) does not match the ISBN consistently returned by multiple
      independent bibliographic sources for this title. AbeBooks, Amazon,
      buscalibre.cl, serindigena.org, and the University of Chile repository all
      record the ISBN as 9789561109124 (ISBN-10: 9561109123) for Foerster González,
      R., Introducción a la religiosidad mapuche (Colección Imagen de Chile),
      Editorial Universitaria, Santiago, 1993. The discrepancy appears to be a
      digit-transposition data-entry error (09315 vs. 09124). Content plausibility
      is not affected — the source is the correct anchor for all 8 cited claims.
    evidence: >
      AbeBooks listing: ISBN 9789561109124 / ISBN-10 9561109123
      (https://www.abebooks.com/9789561109124/Introduccion-religiosidad-mapuche-Coleccion-Imagen-9561109123/plp).
      Amazon listing: same ISBN. University of Chile repository:
      https://repositorio.uchile.cl/handle/2250/122520 (confirms Foerster 1993,
      Editorial Universitaria). University of Chicago catalog record confirms the
      same work at the same publisher and year.
    recommended_fix: >
      Correct the ISBN in sources.yaml from 9789561109315 to 9789561109124
      (ISBN-10: 9561109123) for foerster-religiosidad-mapuche. Update all 8
      footnotes in the claims document accordingly. This is a LOW-priority fix
      on the factual content but MEDIUM priority for bibliographic precision.
      Actionable at post-human-normalize per Ch20 FC-03 / Ch24 FC-03 precedent.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: LOW
    category: identifier-mismatch
    location: >
      sources.yaml entry for course-becoming-mapuche (line 585);
      chapters/27-mapuche-kai-tren.claims.adoc, 2 EVIDENCE tokens citing
      course-becoming-mapuche (lines 102, 118).
    claim_text: >
      "Course, M., *Becoming Mapuche: Person and Ritual in Indigenous Chile*
      (Urbana: University of Illinois Press, 2011; ISBN 9780252078163)"
    problem: >
      The ISBN recorded in sources.yaml and in the chapter's footnotes
      (9780252078163) does not match any confirmed ISBN for this title.
      University of Illinois Press catalogue gives ISBN 9780252036477 (hardcover);
      Amazon and Dussmann give ISBN 9780252078231 (paperback). The number
      9780252078163 appears nowhere in search results. The discrepancy appears
      to be a digit-transposition error (078163 vs. 078231). Content plausibility
      is not affected — Course 2011 is the correct anchor for Mapuche personhood
      and cosmological-ritual framework claims.
    evidence: >
      University of Illinois Press catalogue:
      https://www.press.uillinois.edu/books/catalog/55nhm6yy9780252036477.html
      (hardcover ISBN 9780252036477). Amazon:
      https://www.amazon.com/Becoming-Mapuche-Indigenous-Culture-Millennium/dp/0252078233
      (paperback ISBN 9780252078231). Neither source lists 9780252078163.
    recommended_fix: >
      Confirm the correct ISBN (9780252078231 for paperback or 9780252036477 for
      hardcover) against the University of Illinois Press catalogue and update
      sources.yaml and the 2 footnotes accordingly. Actionable at post-human-
      normalize per Ch20 FC-03 / Ch24 FC-03 precedent.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: LOW
    category: identifier-mismatch
    location: >
      sources.yaml entry for faron-hawks-of-sun (line 597);
      chapters/27-mapuche-kai-tren.claims.adoc, 1 EVIDENCE token citing
      faron-hawks-of-sun (line 112).
    claim_text: >
      "Faron, L.C., *Hawks of the Sun: Mapuche Morality and Its Ritual Attributes*
      (Pittsburgh: University of Pittsburgh Press, 1964; OCLC 247070)"
    problem: >
      The OCLC number in sources.yaml (247070) could not be confirmed as the
      dominant WorldCat record for this title. A WorldCat search returns
      OCLC 320079821 as the record for 'Hawks of the sun: Mapuche morality and
      its ritual attributes' (Faron, 1964, University of Pittsburgh Press). OCLC
      247070 may correspond to a holding-level record at a specific institution or
      a legacy assignment; it could not be verified or disconfirmed from available
      search results. Content plausibility is not affected — Faron 1964 is the
      correct structural-ritual anchor.
    evidence: >
      WorldCat search result: OCLC 320079821 for 'Hawks of the sun : Mapuche
      morality and its ritual attributes' (https://search.worldcat.org/es/title/
      Hawks-of-the-sun-:-Mapuche-morality-and-its-ritual-attributes/oclc/320079821).
      OCLC 247070 not confirmed in search results.
    recommended_fix: >
      Verify OCLC 247070 directly on WorldCat.org or consider using OCLC 320079821
      as the verified dominant bibliographic record identifier. Actionable at
      post-human-normalize per Ch20 FC-03 / Ch24 FC-03 precedent. LOW-priority fix.

  - id: FC-04
    severity: LOW
    category: identifier-mismatch
    location: >
      sources.yaml entry for koessler-ilg-cuentan (line 548-549);
      chapters/27-mapuche-kai-tren.claims.adoc, 10 EVIDENCE tokens citing
      koessler-ilg-cuentan (lines 34, 40, 59, 62, 67, 77, 80, 83, 88, 91).
    claim_text: >
      "Koessler-Ilg, B., *Cuentan los araucanos: Mitos, leyendas y tradiciones*
      (Buenos Aires: Editorial Mar Océano, 1962; OCLC 4124869)"
    problem: >
      Two related concerns: (a) OCLC 4124869 could not be verified via web search
      — no direct WorldCat record for this number was returned. (b) The publisher
      "Editorial Mar Océano, Buenos Aires, 1962" could not be confirmed. Independent
      catalogue records (UNLP FAHCE digital repository, Argentina) show that
      Koessler-Ilg's 1962 Mapuche publication was Tradiciones araucanas (La Plata:
      UNLP / Instituto de Filología, 1962), not Cuentan los araucanos. The
      well-attested Cuentan los araucanos editions are: first edition 1952; 1954
      edition (Buenos Aires: Espasa-Calpe, Colección Austral). It is possible that
      a 1962 Mar Océano edition of Cuentan los araucanos exists in Argentine
      commercial distribution and was assigned OCLC 4124869; but this edition-
      publisher combination could not be independently confirmed from available
      sources. Content plausibility is not affected — Koessler-Ilg's Mapuche
      oral-narrative compilation work (whether titled Cuentan los araucanos or
      Tradiciones araucanas) is the foundational Spanish-language anchor for the
      Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren cycle in either case.
    evidence: >
      UNLP FAHCE digital repository entry: "Koessler-Ilg, Bertha. Tradiciones
      araucanas (1962). La Plata: UNLP. FAHCE. Instituto de Filología."
      (http://www.memoria.fahce.unlp.edu.ar/library?a=d&c=libros&d=Jpm121).
      Multiple search results confirm a 1954 Espasa-Calpe Buenos Aires edition of
      Cuentan los araucanos (Colección Austral). No independent confirmation of
      a 1962 Mar Océano edition of Cuentan los araucanos found in web searches.
    recommended_fix: >
      Before chapter-draft, confirm via WorldCat.org whether OCLC 4124869 corresponds
      to a 1962 Buenos Aires edition of Cuentan los araucanos as recorded in
      sources.yaml, or to the UNLP Tradiciones araucanas (1962). If the 1962 UNLP
      publication is the correct anchor, update sources.yaml title and publisher
      accordingly and clarify in the chapter's footnotes whether the citation is to
      Cuentan los araucanos (1952/1954 edition) or to Tradiciones araucanas (1962).
      If the 1962 Mar Océano edition is confirmed, add a confirmation note to
      sources.yaml. Actionable at post-human-normalize per Ch20 FC-03 / Ch24 FC-03
      precedent. LOW-MEDIUM severity given the content-plausibility pass.

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

    claim: >
      Two serpent-beings — Kai-Kai (marine) and Tren-Tren (mountain) — raise water
      and earth against one another, generating the elevated southern-cone landscape
      and saving the ancestors of the Mapuche from extinction.
    source_id: koessler-ilg-cuentan

  - claim_number: 9
    claim: >
      Kai-Kai (also Kaikai-filu) is the marine serpent of the deep ocean, associated with

      is the area-specialist reading per Foerster 1993. PASS.

  - claim_number: 10
    claim: >
      Tren-Tren (also Trentren-filu) is the mountain serpent of the high land, associated

      counterpart to Kai-Kai, not a hero who slays Kai-Kai; the resolution is a mutual-rising
      stalemate, not a kill-and-eradicate combat-outcome.

      PASS.

# Input file: chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.adoc

```
[[ch-28-bida-wagadu-claims]]
== Bida and the Founding of Wagadu (the Inversion) — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Related-tier (full, inversion anchor) chapter on the Soninke / Mande
// oral tradition in which the seven-headed serpent Bida protects the city
// of Wagadu in exchange for an annual maiden-tribute, and in which the
// killing of Bida by the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote ends Wagadu's
// prosperity, scatters the population, and stops the rains. Per brief
// 28-bida-wagadu.yaml, the chapter is "related (full, inversion anchor)"
// with a target of approximately 5,500 narrative words. Variant handling
// is co-equal: multiple oral recordings (Frobenius 1921, Monteil 1924,
// Dieterlen and successors) differ on Bida's number of heads and on the
// hero's lineage, and the chapter presents the common narrative core
// while explicitly noting recorded-version variation. The chapter opens
// with the Soninke tradition's own framing (covenant, tribute, consequence
// of breaking the covenant, foundation-narrative for the Soninke
// diaspora and the abandonment of the Wagadu / ancient Ghana polity)
// before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and only then
// registers the *inversion* of the chaoskampf typology that the
// narrative exhibits when read against Eurasian combat-myth material.
// The chapter is the present book's principal load-bearing test case
// for Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40 of the
// present volume) and also contributes to Part II C8 (the refusal and
// the appropriation problem; ch. 41).

=== Source overview

The chapter treats the Soninke / Mande oral tradition in which the seven-headed serpent Bida is the protector-spirit of the city of Wagadu and is killed by the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote when the suitor's beloved is taken in the city's annual maiden-tribute, with the killing of Bida triggering the abandonment of Wagadu, the cessation of the rains, and the dispersal of the Soninke population — a narrative in which the slaying of the serpent ends rather than establishes the city's prosperity, inverting the order-establishing outcome of the Eurasian chaoskampf material treated in the preceding chapters of the present book.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml summary on the seven-headed serpent Bida as protector of the Soninke city of Wagadu, on the annual tribute of a young woman, on Mamadi Sefe Dekote's killing of Bida to save his beloved, and on the abandonment of Wagadu / cessation of the rains / dispersal of the people that follows; Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa: Ghana, Mali, and Songhay* (New York: Facts on File; ISBN 9780816052622), ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the narrative core common to the recorded Soninke oral-tradition versions; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press; ISBN 9780253212818), ch. on Soninke traditions, on the Bida / Wagadu narrative as a foundational oral tradition of the Soninke and on the slaying-ends-prosperity narrative pattern that distinguishes the tradition from the Eurasian combat-myth material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The narrative survives in oral-tradition form across the Soninke-speaking communities of the western Sahel (present-day Mauritania, western Mali, and adjacent regions), with no pre-modern written attestation in any African or Arabic-language manuscript tradition; the textual witness situation for the present chapter is therefore the corpus of 19th- and 20th-century ethnographic recordings of oral performances by named Soninke griots and tradition-bearers, supplemented by 20th-century critical-anthology scholarship that synthesises and compares the recorded versions.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the oral-tradition character of the Bida / Wagadu narrative across the Soninke-speaking communities of the western Sahel and on the absence of pre-modern written attestations of the narrative in any African or Arabic-language manuscript tradition; on the chapter's textual witness situation as the corpus of 19th-20th-century ethnographic recordings supplemented by 20th-century critical-anthology scholarship. Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same oral-tradition compositional medium of the narrative and the same recording-history situation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The earliest substantial European-language recording of the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the present chapter's textual witness corpus is Leo Frobenius's *Atlantis* series (volumes IV–VI), published in Jena by Diederichs from 1921 onward, in which Frobenius and his collaborators in the Deutsches Institut für Kulturmorphologie published their German-language recordings of Soninke and other West African oral traditions gathered during the institute's expeditions to the western Sahel — with the Bida / Wagadu material drawn from named Soninke informants whose performances Frobenius's expedition recorded in the 1910s.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on Leo Frobenius's *Atlantis* series (volumes IV–VI; Jena: Diederichs, 1921 onward) as the earliest substantial European-language recording of the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the present chapter's textual witness corpus, on the German-language compilation of Frobenius's recordings, on the Deutsches Institut für Kulturmorphologie expedition context of the 1910s, and on the Soninke informants from whom the *Atlantis* recordings were drawn. Frobenius's *Atlantis* IV–VI is named here in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (the volume is not currently a sources.yaml entry; cited indirectly through Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

A second early recording within the present chapter's textual witness corpus is Charles Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou et l'origine des Soninké," published in 1924 / 1953 within the French ethnographic-administrative scholarly tradition, in which Monteil — writing as a French colonial administrator with extended Soninke-region experience — gathered and published his French-language recording of the Wagadu legend together with a substantial framing-and-commentary apparatus on the legend's place in the oral tradition of the Soninke.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on Charles Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou et l'origine des Soninké" (originally 1924; later expanded edition 1953) as a second early recording of the Bida / Wagadu narrative, on Monteil's status as a French colonial administrator with extended Soninke-region experience, on the French-language compilation of Monteil's recordings, and on the framing-and-commentary apparatus that accompanies the publication. Monteil 1924/1953 is named here in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (not currently a sources.yaml entry; cited indirectly through Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

A further mid-twentieth-century strand of recordings is associated with Germaine Dieterlen and the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition of Mande-area ethnography, which developed an extensive corpus of Soninke and Mande oral-tradition recordings from the 1940s onward and which contributed substantially to the comparative-philological understanding of the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the wider Mande oral-epic tradition.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on Germaine Dieterlen and the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition of Mande-area ethnography from the 1940s onward, on the corpus of Soninke and Mande oral-tradition recordings developed within this tradition, and on Dieterlen's contributions to the comparative-philological understanding of the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the wider Mande oral-epic tradition. Dieterlen and the wider École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition are named here in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The chapter's principal English-language critical-synthesis anchors are David C. Conrad's *Empires of Medieval West Africa: Ghana, Mali, and Songhay* (New York: Facts on File, 2005) and Stephen Belcher's *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1999): Conrad 2005's chapter on the Ghana / Wagadu legend supplies the historical-context framing in which the Bida narrative is set against the Ghana / Wagadu polity of the medieval western Sahel, and Belcher 1999's chapter on Soninke traditions supplies the oral-epic framing in which the narrative's place within the wider Mande / Soninke epic tradition is treated together with the recording-history situation of the 19th- and 20th-century ethnographic corpus.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa: Ghana, Mali, and Songhay* (New York: Facts on File; ISBN 9780816052622), ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the historical-context framing of the Bida narrative against the Ghana / Wagadu polity of the medieval western Sahel; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press; ISBN 9780253212818), ch. on Soninke traditions, on the oral-epic framing of the narrative within the wider Mande / Soninke epic tradition and on the 19th-20th-century ethnographic recording-history; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml sources.primary entries on Conrad 2005 and Belcher 1999 as the chapter's principal anchors.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The historical-polity context of the Bida narrative is the medieval western-Sahelian polity conventionally known in the Arabic-language chronicle tradition as Ghana — distinct from the modern nation-state that bears the same name — which flourished from approximately the eighth century CE through the eleventh-to-twelfth century CE in the region of the upper Niger and upper Senegal river basins, and which the Soninke oral tradition designates as Wagadu, with Conrad 2005's framing treating the legend as a tradition-internal account of the polity's foundation, flourishing, and abandonment from the perspective of the Soninke descendants of the polity's population.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the medieval-western-Sahelian polity conventionally known in the Arabic-language chronicle tradition as Ghana (distinct from the modern nation-state of the same name), on the polity's flourishing from approximately the eighth century CE through the eleventh-to-twelfth century CE in the upper Niger / upper Senegal river basin region, on the Soninke oral-tradition designation Wagadu, and on the legend's status as a tradition-internal account of the polity's foundation-flourishing-abandonment narrative arc; per glossary 'Wagadu' first_mention_gloss on the Soninke city / polity protected by Bida and on the historical ancient Ghana of the Arabic-language chronicle tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The Arabic-language chronicle tradition of the western Sahel — including the seventeenth-century *Tarikh as-Sudan* of Abd al-Rahman al-Sa'di and the related *Tarikh al-Fettash* attributed to Mahmud Kati and his successors — preserves an external historiographical record of the Ghana polity that runs parallel to the Soninke oral tradition of Wagadu, with Conrad 2005's framing treating the two strands (the Arabic-language historiographical record of Ghana and the Soninke oral-tradition narrative of Wagadu / Bida) as complementary windows onto the polity rather than as rival accounts of the same events; the present chapter's witness corpus is the oral-tradition strand, with the Arabic-language chronicle tradition supplying historical-context framing rather than parallel narrative attestation of the Bida material itself.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the Arabic-language chronicle tradition of the western Sahel including the seventeenth-century *Tarikh as-Sudan* of Abd al-Rahman al-Sa'di and the related *Tarikh al-Fettash* attributed to Mahmud Kati and his successors, on the parallel external-historiographical record of the Ghana polity, on the complementary-windows framing under which the Arabic-language and Soninke-oral strands are treated, and on the chapter's witness corpus being the oral-tradition strand with the Arabic-language chronicle tradition supplying historical-context framing rather than parallel narrative attestation of the Bida material. The *Tarikh as-Sudan* and *Tarikh al-Fettash* are named here in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (the chronicles are not currently sources.yaml entries; cited indirectly through Conrad 2005). Cf. also Levtzion's *Ancient Ghana and Mali* (1973) on the Arabic-language chronicle tradition for the Ghana polity, named in body prose per the same pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth tradition is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, whose Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey supplies the long-arc combat-myth typology against which the Bida / Wagadu narrative will be read as the present book's principal *inversion* case; the typological framing — not Mande / Soninke material coverage, which Forsyth does not undertake — is what Forsyth supplies for the chapter's comparative engagement, and the inversion reading depends on having a typology to invert against, which Forsyth's long-arc anchor provides.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic) against which the Bida / Wagadu narrative is read as the present book's principal inversion case; Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Mande / Soninke material directly, and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Mande / Soninke content scholarly coverage. The inversion reading depends on having a typology to invert against, for which Forsyth's long-arc anchor is load-bearing in this chapter even more than in the preceding non-Eurasian related-tier chapters of the present book.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)
