The chapter's textual situation is therefore distinctive within the present book in three further methodological respects: it depends on oral-tradition-to-text recoding rather than on a pre-modern manuscript tradition; the recoding is mediated through 19th- and early-20th-century European ethnographic projects (Frobenius's *Atlantis*; Monteil's colonial-administrative-ethnographic compilation; the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition) whose own framing assumptions filter the recorded material; and there is no surviving pre-modern attestation of the Bida narrative in either African or Arabic-language manuscript form against which the 19th-20th-century recordings can be cross-checked.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the oral-tradition-to-text recoding character of the present chapter's witness corpus rather than a pre-modern manuscript tradition, on the mediation of the recoding through 19th- and early-20th-century European ethnographic projects (Frobenius, Monteil, and the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Dieterlen), on the framing-assumption filtering of the recorded material that this mediation introduces, and on the absence of any surviving pre-modern attestation of the Bida narrative in African or Arabic-language manuscript form against which the recordings could be cross-checked.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

[LACUNA: pre-modern (i.e., pre-19th-century) attestations of the Bida / Wagadu narrative in any written form — African manuscript tradition, Arabic-language chronicle tradition, or European-language traveller's account — are not available, and the surviving textual witness is the corpus of 19th-20th-century ethnographic recordings of Soninke oral-tradition performances by named griots and tradition-bearers (Frobenius's *Atlantis* IV–VI, 1921 onward; Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou," 1924 / 1953; the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest recordings associated with Germaine Dieterlen from the 1940s onward; subsequent 20th-century recordings within Mande-area ethnography). The Arabic-language chronicle tradition of the western Sahel (the *Tarikh as-Sudan*, 17th c., and the *Tarikh al-Fettash*, attributed to Mahmud Kati and successors) preserves a parallel external historiographical record of the Ghana polity but does not attest the Bida narrative itself, and pre-19th-century pre-recording performance contexts (the griot tradition's transmission of the narrative across the Soninke diaspora; the calendrical and royal-ritual contexts in which the narrative was performed in pre-colonial Soninke communities) are partially recoverable through the 19th-20th-century recordings themselves, through the comparative Mande-area ethnographic record, and through the historical Arabic-language chronicle tradition of the Ghana polity. | source: brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the oral-tradition character of the narrative; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the textual witness situation as the 19th-20th-century ethnographic-recording corpus; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the parallel Arabic-language chronicle tradition of the polity. | scholarly_reconstruction: yes — the chapter's narrative reconstruction proceeds entirely within the 19th-20th-century Soninke oral-tradition recording corpus per Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005, treating the recorded versions as a co-equal-variants set per the brief's variants.classification, with Conrad 2005 and Belcher 1999 as the principal interpretive syntheses. Cross-tradition reconstruction is excluded per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" point 1.]

The post-colonial-anthropology filtering caveat that the present chapter must register applies with particular force to the Frobenius and Monteil strands of the recording corpus: where the *Huarochirí Manuscript* of chapter 26 of the present book was compiled within the explicitly anti-idolatry investigation context of Francisco de Ávila and the early-colonial Spanish ecclesiastical campaign, the Bida / Wagadu recordings of Frobenius's *Atlantis* and Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" were compiled within the German and French colonial-ethnographic projects of the early twentieth century, with their own characteristic framing-assumption apparatus; current Mande-studies scholarship including Conrad 2005 and Belcher 1999 reads the early-twentieth-century recordings critically and treats them as a starting point rather than as transparently-faithful records of the pre-colonial oral tradition.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the post-colonial-anthropology filtering caveat applied with particular force to the Frobenius and Monteil strands of the Bida / Wagadu recording corpus, on the German and French colonial-ethnographic project contexts within which the recordings were compiled, on the framing-assumption apparatus that this colonial-ethnographic mediation introduces, and on the critical-reading discipline applied by current Mande-studies scholarship to the early-twentieth-century recordings; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same colonial-ethnographic-mediation and on the chapter's task of treating the recordings critically rather than as transparently-faithful records of the pre-colonial oral tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The chapter is a related-tier (full, inversion anchor) treatment with a target of approximately 5,500 narrative words, presenting the Bida / Wagadu narrative on the tradition's own terms (foundation-narrative, covenant, annual maiden-tribute, killing of the protector-serpent, abandonment of the city, dispersal of the people, cessation of the rains and aetiology of the Saharan dryland environment) before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and only then registering the *inversion* of the chaoskampf typology that the narrative exhibits — with the inversion framing being the chapter's principal contribution to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40 of the present volume) per brief special_instructions and per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the related-tier (full, inversion anchor) classification, the 5,500-word target, and the special_instructions requirement that the chapter open with the Soninke narrative on its own terms (covenant, tribute, consequence of breaking the covenant) before invoking the inversion-of-chaoskampf frame; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion-problem comparative chapter (ch. 40) for which the present chapter is the principal load-bearing test case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

=== The Soninke, Wagadu, and the historical Ghana polity

The Soninke (also recorded as Soninké, Sarakolé, or Marka in different ethnographic and historical-administrative traditions) are a Mande-speaking population of the western Sahel whose historical heartland is the upper Senegal / upper Niger river basin region of present-day Mauritania, western Mali, and adjacent parts of Senegal, and whose oral tradition designates the medieval western-Sahelian polity that flourished there as Wagadu — the same polity known in the Arabic-language chronicle tradition as Ghana.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa: Ghana, Mali, and Songhay*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the Soninke (Soninké, Sarakolé, Marka in different ethnographic and historical-administrative traditions) as a Mande-speaking population of the western Sahel, on their historical heartland in the upper Senegal / upper Niger river basin region of present-day Mauritania, western Mali, and adjacent parts of Senegal, and on the Soninke oral-tradition designation of the medieval polity as Wagadu / Arabic-language Ghana.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The historical Ghana / Wagadu polity flourished from approximately the eighth century CE through the eleventh-to-twelfth century CE — the conventional periodisation in current Mande-studies scholarship — with its political and economic significance grounded in its position athwart the trans-Saharan trade routes connecting the western Sahel with the Maghreb, and particularly in its control of the gold-producing regions south and southwest of the polity's heartland; the polity's decline in the eleventh-to-twelfth century is conventionally connected with the Almoravid pressures of the late eleventh century and with the subsequent reorganisation of the western-Sahelian political landscape under the rising Mali polity of the thirteenth century onward.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the historical periodisation of the Ghana / Wagadu polity from approximately the eighth century CE through the eleventh-to-twelfth century CE, on the polity's position athwart the trans-Saharan trade routes connecting the western Sahel with the Maghreb, on its control of the gold-producing regions south and southwest of the heartland, and on the conventional connection of the polity's decline with the Almoravid pressures of the late eleventh century and with the subsequent rise of the Mali polity from the thirteenth century onward.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The Soninke oral tradition's own framing of the Wagadu narrative is foundational and aetiological: the narrative is told as the founding charter of the Soninke as a people, as the explanation of their dispersal across the western Sahel and beyond from a once-prosperous heartland, and as the aetiology of the encroachment of the Sahara on the formerly-watered region of the polity's territory; the narrative is therefore a connected foundation-story rather than a primarily cosmogonic narrative, and the cosmos within which it unfolds is the already-existing world of the western Sahel rather than a cosmogonically-emerging cosmos.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the Soninke oral tradition's framing of the Wagadu narrative as the founding charter of the Soninke as a people, as the explanation of their dispersal across the western Sahel and beyond, and as the aetiology of the encroachment of the Sahara on the formerly-watered region; on the narrative's character as a connected foundation-story rather than a primarily cosmogonic narrative, and on the already-existing-world cosmographic framing within which the narrative unfolds; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the foundation-narrative, aetiological, and moral-didactic functions of the narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The narrative's principal protagonists are: Bida, the seven-headed serpent who is the protector-spirit of Wagadu and to whom the city's prosperity is bound by covenant; the rulers of Wagadu, who maintain the covenant on the city's behalf; the Soninke population of the city, on whose ritual maintenance of the annual tribute the city's prosperity depends; the maiden delivered each year as the tribute (her name and lineage varying by recorded version); the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote, who breaks the covenant by killing Bida to save his beloved; and the displaced Soninke population, whose dispersal across the western Sahel after the killing supplies the foundation-narrative for the Soninke diaspora.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the principal protagonists of the narrative — Bida the seven-headed serpent-protector of Wagadu, the rulers of Wagadu maintaining the covenant, the Soninke population on whose ritual maintenance of the annual tribute the city's prosperity depends, the maiden delivered each year as the tribute (with her name and lineage varying by recorded version), the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote breaking the covenant by killing Bida, and the displaced Soninke population whose dispersal supplies the foundation-narrative for the Soninke diaspora; per glossary 'Bida', 'Wagadu', 'Mamadi Sefe Dekote' first_mention_glosses on the principal protagonists.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

=== The covenant: Bida as protector and the annual tribute

The covenant between Bida and the Soninke of Wagadu is the narrative's load-bearing structural premise: in the recorded versions, the seven-headed serpent Bida agrees to protect the city, to grant prosperity in the form of regular rains and (in many versions) an annual rain of gold, in exchange for a yearly tribute of the most beautiful maiden of the community, whose delivery to Bida's well or pool is the ritual act through which the covenant is maintained year by year.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the covenant between Bida and the Soninke of Wagadu as the narrative's load-bearing structural premise; on the seven-headed serpent's protection of the city, on the prosperity granted in the form of regular rains and (in many versions) an annual rain of gold, on the yearly tribute of the most beautiful maiden of the community, and on the delivery to Bida's well or pool as the ritual act through which the covenant is maintained year by year; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same covenant-and-annual-tribute structural premise.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The covenant's structural function in the narrative is therefore order-maintenance rather than order-establishment: the cosmos and the city are already in place when the narrative begins, the covenant maintains the prosperity of the city against the always-present threat of disorder (drought, scattering, the encroachment of the desert), and the annual maiden-tribute is the ritual practice through which the maintaining-of-order is performed by the human community in concert with the protector-serpent — a structural arrangement that places the covenant within the *cyclical-maintenance* register of order-making rather than the *cosmogonic* or *sovereignty-establishing* registers per scope.md §"Subject definition".footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the covenant's structural function as order-maintenance rather than order-establishment, on the already-in-place cosmos-and-city framing of the narrative, on the maintaining-of-prosperity-against-always-present-threat-of-disorder pattern, and on the annual maiden-tribute as the ritual practice through which the maintaining-of-order is performed; per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the cyclical-maintenance register of order-making (cf. Ra/Apep, Illuyanka in the *purulli* festival) as distinct from the cosmogonic and sovereignty-establishing registers.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

Bida's seven-headedness is the most widely attested form of the protector-serpent across the recorded versions, although the recorded-version corpus shows variation in the number of heads (some versions reduce the count to a single head; some versions intermediate between three and nine), with the seven-fold form being the form privileged in Conrad 2005's and Belcher 1999's English-language critical-synthesis treatments and in the glossary-locked rendering of the present book.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the seven-headed form of Bida as the most widely attested form of the protector-serpent across the recorded versions, on the variation across the corpus (single-headed forms; intermediate three-to-nine forms), and on the seven-fold form's privilege in the English-language critical-synthesis treatments; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same seven-headed form privilege; per glossary 'Bida' first_mention_gloss on the seven-headed serpent-protector form.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

Bida's spatial association in the narrative is with a specific watering-place — variously rendered as a well, a pool, a spring, or (in some versions) a sacred grove containing such a feature — within or adjacent to Wagadu, with the watering-place being the narrative's site of the annual tribute and the structural anchor of Bida's protective presence in the city; the water-association is load-bearing for the narrative's aetiological function, since the cessation of the rains after Bida's death is narratively connected with the loss of the protector-spirit's water-anchored presence in the city.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on Bida's spatial association in the narrative with a specific watering-place (well, pool, spring, or sacred grove containing such a feature) within or adjacent to Wagadu, on the watering-place as the site of the annual tribute and the structural anchor of Bida's protective presence in the city, and on the load-bearing connection between the water-association and the narrative's aetiological function (cessation of the rains after Bida's death narratively connected with the loss of the water-anchored protective presence).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The annual tribute's character as the *most beautiful maiden* of the community — rather than (e.g.) any maiden of the appropriate age, or a sacrificial victim drawn by lot — is structurally important for the narrative's subsequent inflection: in the year of the killing, the maiden whose turn has come is the beloved of a particular suitor, and the suitor's grief and refusal to accept the loss is the narrative's precipitating-crisis-moment that motivates the breaking of the covenant.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the annual tribute's character as the most beautiful maiden of the community rather than any maiden of the appropriate age or a sacrificial victim drawn by lot, on the structural-narrative importance of the most-beautiful-maiden specification for the subsequent inflection of the narrative, and on the suitor's grief and refusal to accept the loss as the narrative's precipitating-crisis-moment that motivates the breaking of the covenant.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The covenant's antiquity within the narrative is presented as foundational: the recorded versions place the establishment of the covenant at or near the founding of Wagadu itself, with the city's flourishing across many generations under the covenant's terms being the narrative's framing of the long pre-killing era of the polity, and the killing of Bida placing the end of the covenant at the moment of the city's abandonment rather than at the moment of its founding.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the covenant's antiquity within the narrative as foundational, on the placement of the establishment of the covenant at or near the founding of Wagadu itself, on the city's flourishing across many generations under the covenant's terms as the narrative's framing of the long pre-killing era, and on the placement of the killing at the moment of the city's abandonment rather than at the moment of its founding.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

=== Mamadi Sefe Dekote and the killing of Bida

In the year of the killing, the maiden whose turn has come for the annual tribute is the betrothed (or, in some versions, the most-favoured beloved) of the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote, whose name is variously translated in the recorded versions as "Mamadi the Taciturn" or "Mamadi of Few Words" in conventional gloss; Mamadi's lineage is given in some recorded versions as descending from the rulers of the city, and in other versions as the head of one of the city's principal warrior-lineages, with the specifics of the lineage being among the principal points of variation across the recorded-version corpus.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the year of the killing as the year in which the maiden whose turn has come is the betrothed or most-favoured beloved of Mamadi Sefe Dekote, on the conventional gloss of Mamadi's name as "Mamadi the Taciturn" or "Mamadi of Few Words," on the variation in his lineage across the recorded versions (descending from the rulers in some, head of a principal warrior-lineage in others), and on lineage-variation as among the principal points of variation across the recorded-version corpus; per glossary 'Mamadi Sefe Dekote' first_mention_gloss on the suitor who kills Bida.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

Mamadi's response to the impending tribute is to refuse the loss of his beloved and to resolve to kill Bida rather than allow the maiden to be delivered to the protector-serpent; in the recorded versions, this resolution is undertaken in defiance of the rulers of the city, of the elders of the maiden's family, and (in some versions) of the explicit advice of his beloved herself, who urges him to accept the tribute as the price of the city's continued prosperity.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on Mamadi's response to the impending tribute as refusal of the loss of his beloved and resolution to kill Bida, on the resolution being undertaken in defiance of the rulers of the city and of the elders of the maiden's family, and (in some recorded versions) on the explicit advice of his beloved herself urging him to accept the tribute as the price of the city's continued prosperity.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The killing itself, in the most widely attested form of the narrative, takes place at Bida's watering-place when the protector-serpent emerges to receive the annual tribute: Mamadi conceals himself near the watering-place with his sword, waits until the serpent emerges and lowers its heads to take the maiden, and strikes the heads from the serpent's body in succession — with the head-by-head pattern of the killing being a load-bearing narrative feature in the seven-headed renderings, and with the recorded versions varying in the number of strokes and in the response of the serpent's regenerating heads to each stroke.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the killing's setting at Bida's watering-place at the moment of the annual tribute, on Mamadi's concealment near the watering-place with his sword, on the waiting for the serpent to emerge and lower its heads, on the head-by-head pattern of the killing as a load-bearing narrative feature in the seven-headed renderings, and on the variation across recorded versions in the number of strokes and in the response of the serpent's regenerating heads.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

In the most widely attested form of the narrative, Bida's last head — or, in some renderings, the dying serpent itself — pronounces a curse upon Wagadu before the final stroke, foretelling the cessation of the rains, the encroachment of the desert, the abandonment of the city, and the dispersal of the Soninke across the western Sahel; the curse-pronouncement is the narrative's structural device that converts the killing-of-the-protector into the aetiology of the polity's end and of the Soninke diaspora.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the most widely attested form of the narrative in which Bida's last head — or, in some renderings, the dying serpent itself — pronounces a curse upon Wagadu before the final stroke, on the curse's content (cessation of the rains, encroachment of the desert, abandonment of the city, dispersal of the Soninke across the western Sahel), and on the curse-pronouncement as the narrative's structural device that converts the killing-of-the-protector into the aetiology of the polity's end and of the Soninke diaspora.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The maiden is, in the most widely attested versions, saved by the killing — Mamadi's intervention does prevent her from being devoured by the serpent — but the narrative does not present this rescue as redemptive in the longer arc: the rescue is achieved at the cost of the city's prosperity, the maiden's saving is locally successful but structurally insufficient against the loss of the covenant, and the narrative's moral-didactic function (per brief cultural_relevance.function) is precisely to register that the cost of breaking the covenant exceeds the benefit of the immediate rescue.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the maiden's being saved by the killing in the most widely attested versions, on Mamadi's intervention as preventing the immediate devouring, on the rescue's structural insufficiency against the loss of the covenant, and on the narrative's moral-didactic function as registering that the cost of breaking the covenant exceeds the benefit of the immediate rescue; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the moral-didactic function (the cost of breaking a covenant).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

[VARIANT: primary=co-equal recorded versions per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml variants.classification, treating the principal recorded ethnographic versions (Frobenius's *Atlantis* IV–VI 1921 onward; Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" 1924 / 1953; the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Germaine Dieterlen from the 1940s onward; subsequent 20th-century recordings within Mande-area ethnography) as a co-equal-variants set in which differences in Bida's number of heads (seven in the dominant rendering; reduced or intermediate forms in other renderings), in Mamadi's lineage (descending from the city's rulers in some recordings; head of a principal warrior-lineage in others), and in further narrative details (the maiden's name; the precise content of Bida's dying curse; the number of strokes in the killing) are presented as legitimate variant attestations rather than as deviations from a postulated original | alts=alternative variant treatments are available — for example, treating one of the recorded strands (e.g., Frobenius's *Atlantis* recordings, or the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest recordings) as the chapter's privileged primary source with the others as supplementary, or attempting to reconstruct an original-version skeleton from the convergences across the recorded corpus | chosen: co-equal-recorded-versions per the brief's variants.classification | reason: per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml variants.classification: co-equal; the chapter presents the common narrative core with explicit noting of regional and recorded-version variation, treating the recorded ethnographic strands as a co-equal-variants set rather than privileging any one strand or attempting to reconstruct an underlying original — a treatment that is also methodologically appropriate to oral-tradition material in which no pre-recording original is recoverable behind the recordings.]

The post-killing narrative, in the most widely attested form, immediately enters the aetiological-and-foundation register: the rains cease over Wagadu in the year of the killing or in the years immediately following, the gold (in versions in which Bida's protection includes the annual rain of gold) ceases to fall, the wells and pools of the city dry up, the city becomes uninhabitable, and the Soninke population scatters across the western Sahel and beyond — with the dispersal supplying the narrative's aetiology of the Soninke diaspora and of the present-day distribution of Soninke-speaking communities across the upper Senegal / upper Niger river basin region.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the post-killing narrative in the most widely attested form as immediate entry into the aetiological-and-foundation register, on the cessation of the rains over Wagadu in the year of the killing or in the years immediately following, on the cessation of the (versional) annual rain of gold, on the drying of wells and pools, on the city's becoming uninhabitable, on the scattering of the Soninke population across the western Sahel and beyond, and on the dispersal as the narrative's aetiology of the Soninke diaspora and of the present-day distribution of Soninke-speaking communities; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml summary on the abandonment-rains-cease-people-scatter pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The narrative's connection between the killing of Bida and the encroachment of the Sahara on the formerly-watered region of Wagadu is one of the recorded tradition's most striking aetiological elements: the desert's southward expansion, which current paleoclimatic and historical-geographic scholarship treats as a gradual process across the medieval and post-medieval centuries with multiple contributing factors, is presented within the Soninke oral tradition as a direct consequence of the killing of the protector-serpent and the breaking of the covenant — supplying an indigenous narrative aetiology for an environmental change that the polity and its successors observed across the centuries.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the narrative's connection between the killing of Bida and the encroachment of the Sahara on the formerly-watered region of Wagadu as one of the recorded tradition's most striking aetiological elements, on the desert's southward expansion as a gradual process across the medieval and post-medieval centuries within current paleoclimatic and historical-geographic scholarship, and on the narrative's supplying an indigenous aetiology for an environmental change that the polity and its successors observed across the centuries; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the aetiological function of the rains' cessation and the Sahara's spread.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

Mamadi Sefe Dekote's own fate after the killing varies across the recorded versions: in some versions he survives the killing, departs Wagadu with his beloved and a portion of the population, and becomes the founder of a successor-community elsewhere in the western Sahel; in other versions he is killed in the aftermath, either by the dying serpent's poison, by the rulers of the city in retribution for the breaking of the covenant, or by his own grief; the variation in his fate is among the principal points of recorded-version variation alongside the variations in his lineage and in the maiden's identity.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the variation in Mamadi Sefe Dekote's fate across the recorded versions: survival and departure from Wagadu with his beloved and a portion of the population to found a successor-community in some versions; killing in the aftermath by the dying serpent's poison, by the rulers of the city in retribution, or by his own grief in others; and on this variation as among the principal points of recorded-version variation alongside the variations in his lineage and in the maiden's identity.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

=== The covenant-and-consequence reading: the tradition's own framing

The Soninke oral-tradition's own framing of the narrative is, per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels, *covenant-and-consequence* rather than *cosmogonic-reversal*: the narrative is told as the story of a covenant well-kept across many generations, a covenant broken in a single generation by a single act of unsanctioned individual heroism, and the consequence of the breaking — the abandonment of the city, the dispersal of the people, the cessation of the rains — as the moral-didactic kernel that the narrative communicates to its hearers; the inversion-of-chaoskampf reading is a 20th-century-comparativist overlay that the tradition's own framing does not anticipate.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the Soninke tradition's framing of the narrative as covenant-and-consequence rather than cosmogonic-reversal, on the inversion-of-chaoskampf reading as a 20th-century comparativist overlay rather than an internal feature of the tradition, and on the chapter's task of opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the narrative's moral-didactic kernel as the consequence-of-breaking-the-covenant and on the well-kept-then-broken covenant pattern as the tradition's own narrative-structural framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The narrative's moral-didactic register within the Soninke tradition is therefore directed at the audience of the performance rather than at any cosmogonic question: the griot's audience is invited to consider the cost of breaking a covenant for the sake of an immediate good, to feel the gravity of Mamadi's choice and the tragic-didactic register of the consequence, and to recognise in the narrative the founding of the Soninke people through the very loss of Wagadu — with the foundation-narrative function and the moral-didactic function being intertwined in the narrative's tradition-internal performance context.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the narrative's moral-didactic register within the Soninke tradition as directed at the audience of the performance rather than at any cosmogonic question, on the griot's audience being invited to consider the cost of breaking a covenant for the sake of an immediate good and to feel the gravity of Mamadi's choice and the tragic-didactic register of the consequence, and on the foundation-narrative function and moral-didactic function as intertwined in the narrative's tradition-internal performance context; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the moral-didactic function (the cost of breaking a covenant) alongside the foundation-narrative function (origin of the Soninke diaspora and the end of Wagadu / ancient Ghana).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

Comparable covenant-serpent narrative patterns are attested across other West African oral traditions — including stories in which a protective serpent or other powerful being maintains the prosperity of a community in exchange for ritual observances, and in which the breaking of the covenant by an unsanctioned individual act ends the community's prosperity — with the chapter's task being to register the existence of these comparable patterns without assimilating them into a single comparative category, in line with the brief's cross_cultural_parallels guidance.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions and ch. on related West African oral-epic traditions, on comparable covenant-serpent narrative patterns attested across other West African oral traditions in which a protective serpent or other powerful being maintains community prosperity in exchange for ritual observances and in which the breaking of the covenant ends the prosperity, and on the chapter's task of registering these comparable patterns without assimilating them into a single comparative category; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on similar covenant-serpent patterns in other West African traditions and on flagging without assimilating.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

=== The inversion of the chaoskampf typology

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Bida / Wagadu narrative as a *structural inversion* of the Eurasian chaoskampf typology — in which the protector-serpent's slaying ENDS rather than ESTABLISHES the city's prosperity, the human-and-divine cycle of annual maiden-tribute is the *order-maintenance* practice that the killing breaks rather than the *order-establishing* practice that the killing inaugurates, and the hero's act of slaying the monstrous-serpentine adversary is the narrative's *anti-foundational* moment rather than its *foundational* moment — is a synthesis of the brief's framing per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml summary and special_instructions, of Belcher 1999's and Conrad 2005's English-language critical-synthesis treatments, and of scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion entry on Bida as the case "where slaying the serpent *destroys* prosperity rather than establishing it"; the inversion reading is held in the chapter as the chapter's principal load-bearing comparative reading and as the present book's principal load-bearing test case for Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40 of the present volume). | basis: brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml summary explicitly registers the slaying-ends-prosperity-not-establishes-it inversion as the structural feature that distinguishes the Bida narrative from the Eurasian chaoskampf material; brief special_instructions classifies the chapter as the "PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE FOR PART II C7"; scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion lists Bida as the inversion case explicitly; Conrad 2005's and Belcher 1999's narrative-treatment apparatus develop the slaying-ends-the-city pattern at length, although in the tradition's own register of covenant-and-consequence rather than as inversion-of-chaoskampf-typology in the comparativist register. | risk: the inversion reading is itself a comparativist overlay on the Soninke tradition's own framing of the narrative as covenant-and-consequence (per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the inverse-chaoskampf reading as a 20th-century comparativist frame); the chapter's task is to hold the inversion reading as a load-bearing comparative reading while explicitly registering that the tradition's own framing does not anticipate it, and that the comparativist inversion-frame can obscure as much as it reveals (per brief special_instructions on the Part II C7 argument that "inversion is a comparativist artifact that can obscure more than it reveals"). The fact-check stage should confirm that the inversion reading is supported by the brief's framing and by Conrad 2005's and Belcher 1999's treatment apparatus at the level of the chapter's claims, recognising that the inversion frame itself is the load-bearing methodological move that Part II C7 is to investigate.]

The inversion of the chaoskampf typology that the Bida / Wagadu narrative exhibits operates at the level of every load-bearing structural feature of the Eurasian combat-myth pattern: where the chaoskampf-tradition adversary (Tiamat, Vṛtra, Apep, Lôtan, Aži Dahāka, Jörmungandr, Typhon) is the *adversary* of the cosmos or the polity, Bida is the *protector* of the polity; where the Eurasian combat-myth slaying *establishes* cosmic order or sovereignty, the slaying of Bida *destroys* the polity's prosperity; where the human-and-divine cycle of ritual observance in the cyclical-maintenance register (the *purulli* festival of Illuyanka, the daily Apep-overthrowing ritual) is the *order-maintenance* practice that *re-enacts* the Eurasian combat as a means of preserving order, the annual maiden-tribute of the Bida narrative is the *order-maintenance* practice that the killing *breaks* rather than re-enacts.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four chaoskampf order-making registers (cosmogonic, sovereignty, cyclical-maintenance, eschatological); per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion on Bida as the inversion case where slaying the serpent destroys prosperity rather than establishing it; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, and Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the structural features of the Bida narrative that distinguish it from the Eurasian combat-myth pattern at the levels of the protector-versus-adversary status of the serpent, the destroying-versus-establishing outcome of the killing, and the broken-versus-re-enacted character of the order-maintenance ritual practice.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The inversion's structural depth — operating at *every* load-bearing feature of the chaoskampf pattern rather than at one or another isolated feature — is what distinguishes the Bida / Wagadu narrative from the related-tier non-Eurasian cases of the present book that *transform* the chaoskampf category (the Pariacaca cycle of chapter 26, where the cycle is wak'a-succession rather than cosmos-versus-chaos combat; the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi episode of chapter 23, where the narrative is read as sword-etiology and metallurgical allegory) and that *refuse* the chaoskampf category (the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material of chapter 32, where the Anishinaabe specialist tradition rejects the combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing); the Bida narrative is the present book's principal *inversion* case in the strong sense that the Eurasian chaoskampf pattern's structural features are present but with their valences reversed at every load-bearing point.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion problem (Bida anchor) and on the distinction between inversion (structural pattern present with valences reversed) and transformation (chaoskampf category transformed when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing); per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's status as the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE FOR PART II C7 inversion-problem; per the Ch26 brief on the Pariacaca cycle as a transformation rather than inversion case; per the Ch23 brief on the Susanoo / Orochi narrative as another transformation case (sword-etiology and metallurgical-allegory reading); per the Ch32 brief on the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material as a refusal case in the strong sense.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The inversion reading must, however, be held as a *comparativist* reading rather than as a tradition-internal structural feature: the Soninke tradition's own framing of the narrative is covenant-and-consequence (per the preceding section), the inversion-of-chaoskampf is a 20th-century-Western comparativist overlay, and the chapter's load-bearing methodological discipline is to hold the comparativist inversion-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation — with the heuristic's value being precisely that it can register what the Eurasian chaoskampf pattern's structural assumptions look like *from outside* when held against a structurally-symmetric counter-example, while remaining a 20th-century-Western reading of the narrative rather than a Soninke reading.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the inverse-chaoskampf reading as a 20th-century comparativist frame and on the Soninke tradition's framing as covenant-and-consequence rather than cosmogonic-reversal; per brief special_instructions on the chapter setting up the Part II C7 argument that "inversion is a comparativist artifact that can obscure more than it reveals"; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3 on opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative category, and on holding the comparative category loosely where the tradition's own framing does not anticipate it.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: Mande-area / West-African-studies area specialists — including David C. Conrad in his *Empires of Medieval West Africa* (Conrad 2005) and his wider corpus of Mande-tradition scholarship; Stephen Belcher in his *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Belcher 1999) and his wider corpus of West African oral-epic scholarship; Thomas A. Hale in his *Griots and Griottes* (Hale 1998) and his work on Mande oral-tradition performance; Charles Bird in his pioneering Mande oral-epic-textual scholarship; the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Germaine Dieterlen; and Nehemia Levtzion in his *Ancient Ghana and Mali* (Levtzion 1973) on the Arabic-language chronicle tradition for the Ghana polity — read the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the registers of Mande oral-epic performance, of the Soninke foundation-narrative tradition, and of the historical Ghana / Wagadu polity, rather than within the chaoskampf-typology register imported from Gunkel-tradition combat-myth scholarship. The area-specialist objection to the chaoskampf typology's application to the Bida narrative does not, however, take the form of rejecting the combat-myth framing as such (as the Naumann / Isomae / Como objection does for the Susanoo / Orochi material in chapter 23, where the area-specialists object to the combat-myth framing in favour of a metallurgical-allegory reading) or of rejecting the combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing entirely (as the Hallowell / Johnston / T. Smith / Vizenor objection does for the Anishinaabe Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material in chapter 32, where the area-specialists insist on a necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing): the Mande-area tradition's framing of the narrative as covenant-and-consequence does accommodate the recognition of a serpent-and-hero combat at the narrative's centre, since the combat is unambiguously present in the recorded versions; the area-specialist objection is rather to the *typological assimilation* of the Bida combat to the Eurasian chaoskampf category — to which the combat's structural features are inverted at every load-bearing point — rather than to the recognition of combat per se. The chapter's load-bearing methodological move is therefore to use the inversion reading as the heuristic that registers exactly this objection: the narrative is recognised as featuring serpent-and-hero combat (the area-specialist framing accommodates this), but the typology of order-establishment that the chaoskampf category presupposes is registered as inverted in the Bida case (the area-specialist framing of covenant-and-consequence aligns with the chapter's inversion-frame on this structural-typological question, even if the registers in which the framings are stated differ between tradition-internal moral-didactic and Western-comparativist-typological). The area-specialist objection is on the spectrum of objection-strengths between the Naumann objection in Ch23 (objection to combat-myth framing as such, in favour of metallurgical allegory) and the Pariacaca-cycle Salomon-Urioste-Taylor-Dean objection in Ch26 (objection to chaos-order framing but not to combat-myth framing as such, in favour of wak'a-succession): the Mande-area objection is closer to the Pariacaca-cycle case (objection to typological-assimilation rather than to combat-myth-recognition as such) than to the Susanoo case, but is structurally distinctive in that the area-specialist tradition's own framing (covenant-and-consequence) and the chapter's comparative framing (inversion of chaoskampf) converge on the same structural recognition that the slaying-ENDS-prosperity feature is load-bearing for the narrative — even though the framings reach this convergence by different routes. Forwarded to Part II C7 (inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40 of the present volume) as the principal load-bearing test case, with the methodological discipline that the inversion frame is to be held as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection and that Part II C7 is to investigate the question of whether the inversion frame ultimately obscures more than it reveals about the Soninke tradition's own framing — per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions. Forwarded to Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation problem; ch. 41) for the comparative-engagement work of holding the chaoskampf typology loosely where Mande-area specialists object to its application. Conrad and Belcher are the chapter's principal anchors and named in the first factual pass per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 1; Hale, Bird, and Levtzion are named in body prose at this marker per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (Hale's *Griots and Griottes*, Indiana University Press 1998; Bird's Mande oral-epic publications; Levtzion's *Ancient Ghana and Mali*, Methuen 1973 — none currently in sources.yaml; deferred to inventory-audit per Ch23 FC-02 and Ch26 FC-02 named-but-flagged precedent).]

The chapter's place within the present book's chaoskampf typology is — as the brief's "related (full, inversion anchor)" tier classification specifies — a related-tier (rather than core-tier) case that contributes the load-bearing structural counter-example against which the chaoskampf typology's load-bearing assumptions can be tested; the chapter's structural significance for the present book is therefore disproportionate to its tier classification, since the related-tier classification (correct per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" because the area-specialist consensus does not assimilate the narrative to chaoskampf in the strong sense) is consistent with the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for Part II C7.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the cosmogonic-combat work is weaker or where area-specialists qualify the comparativist reading; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml chapter_type "related (full, inversion anchor)" classification; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for the inversion / transformation problem comparative chapter (ch. 40); per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's status as the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE FOR PART II C7.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

=== Typology, area-specialist framing, and forward-links

The Bida / Wagadu narrative's combat-and-killing structural pattern is recognisable across the recorded versions: a hero (Mamadi Sefe Dekote) confronts a serpentine adversary (the seven-headed Bida) in a defined combat-encounter (at Bida's watering-place, at the moment of the annual tribute), wields a weapon (the sword) against the serpent's heads in a head-by-head pattern of strokes, and successfully completes the killing — with the combat-pattern having every surface-level feature of the Eurasian hero-slay-serpent narrative, and with the structural-typological inversion operating at the level of *what the killing accomplishes* rather than at the level of *what the killing is*.footnote:[Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, and Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the combat-and-killing structural pattern of the narrative as recognisable across the recorded versions, on the surface-level features of the encounter-weapon-stroke-killing sequence as having every Eurasian hero-slay-serpent feature, and on the structural-typological inversion as operating at the level of what the killing accomplishes (destruction of the polity rather than establishment of cosmic order or sovereignty) rather than at the level of what the killing is (a combat-and-killing in the standard hero-and-serpent register).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C7 (the comparative chapter on the inversion / transformation problem, ch. 40 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes the *inversion* sub-problem proper as its load-bearing test case: a story in which the chaoskampf category's structural features are present but with their valences inverted at every load-bearing point, with the chapter's task being to hold the inversion frame as the heuristic that registers exactly this structural feature while also flagging that the inversion frame is a 20th-century-Western comparativist overlay on the Soninke tradition's own covenant-and-consequence framing.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem) as the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion-and-transformation comparative chapter; per the chapter's status as the present book's principal load-bearing test case for the inversion sub-problem proper, distinguished from the transformation sub-problem cases of chapters 23 (Susanoo / Orochi) and 26 (Pariacaca cycle).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to Part II C8 (the comparative chapter on the refusal and the appropriation problem, ch. 41 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes a working example of the *area-specialist objection to typological-assimilation* sub-problem: the Mande-area tradition (Conrad, Belcher, Hale, Bird, Dieterlen, Levtzion) reads the narrative within the registers of Mande oral-epic performance and Soninke foundation-narrative rather than within the chaoskampf-typology register, and the chapter's appropriation-protocol-compliant treatment is to use the inversion frame as the heuristic that registers the area-specialist objection rather than as a typology that imposes the Eurasian pattern on the Mande-area material.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy (Conrad and Belcher named in the first factual pass; the wider Mande-area tradition acknowledged); per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement and on the comparative chapter's task of addressing area-specialist objections rather than papering over them; per the chapter's [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker registering the Mande-area objection to typological-assimilation; per the spectrum-of-objection-strengths positioning between the Susanoo / Orochi case (Ch23) and the Pariacaca-cycle case (Ch26).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The chapter's contribution to the methods sidebar (chapter 33 of the present volume, between Part I and Part II) is on three methodological points: the oral-tradition-to-text recoding situation (the Bida / Wagadu narrative being a 19th-20th-century ethnographic-recording corpus rather than a pre-modern manuscript tradition, which the chapter handles via the [LACUNA] marker on pre-modern attestations and via continuous methodological registration of the colonial-ethnographic mediation); the inversion-frame methodological move (the use of the comparativist inversion-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation, which the chapter handles via the [INFERENCE] marker on the inversion reading and via the explicit comparativist-overlay registration in body prose); and the area-specialist citation policy as applied to non-Eurasian non-textual traditions (which the chapter handles via the [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker and via the named-but-flagged secondary-specialists pattern for Frobenius, Monteil, Dieterlen, Hale, Bird, and Levtzion alongside the Conrad and Belcher anchors).footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy and on the colonial-ethnographic recording-history situation; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement, and on §"Reconstruction policy" point 1 on no cross-tradition reconstruction; chapter 33 methods sidebar (per toc.yaml) as the chapter that articulates these methodological points at full length; the chapter's status as the present book's principal load-bearing test case for the inversion-frame methodological move makes it a central reference point for the methods sidebar's discussion of comparativist heuristics held at appropriate distance from area-specialist consensus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to chapter 26 on Pariacaca and the amaru, with which the present chapter shares the related-tier non-Eurasian-cluster classification and the area-specialist-objection registration pattern, but contrasts in the substance of the area-specialist objection: the Pariacaca cycle is a *transformation* case in which the chaoskampf category transforms into wak'a-succession when the area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing, while the Bida / Wagadu narrative is an *inversion* case in which the chaoskampf category's structural features are present but with their valences reversed at every load-bearing point — the two cases between them anchoring the *transformation* and *inversion* sub-problems of Part II C7 from different non-Eurasian-cluster locations within the present book.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion-and-transformation comparative chapter; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml on the related-tier (full) classification of the Pariacaca cycle on the basis of the area-specialist wak'a-succession-not-chaoskampf reading (the transformation case); per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the related-tier (full, inversion anchor) classification of the Bida / Wagadu narrative on the basis of the slaying-ends-prosperity inversion reading (the inversion case); per the structural distinction between transformation (category transforms when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing) and inversion (category's structural features present with valences reversed) developed across the two chapters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The chapter's secondary back-links within the present book are to chapter 24 (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec) and chapter 25 (the Mesoamerican cluster) on the Americas-cluster contrast — Huitzilopochtli's slaying of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua at Coatepec is a *paradigmatic* establishment-of-sovereignty combat in the chaoskampf register (per the area-specialist consensus reading developed in Ch24), while the Bida narrative is an inversion case at the opposite typological pole — and to the forward chapter 27 on the Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren material (the Pacific Andes / South Cone non-Eurasian comparandum), where the cosmography-and-flood pattern of the Mapuche material supplies a further non-Eurasian-cluster comparison that Part II C7 will engage; the back-link to the Mapuche material is forward in chapter-number but back in geographic-cluster sequence, and is registered without consultation of the Mapuche-chapter material per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 10 (Americas) and cluster 11 (Africa) on the chapter ordering of 24 (Huitzilopochtli core-tier), 25 (Mesoamerican family related-tier), 26 (Pariacaca related-tier), 27 (Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren related-family), 28 (Bida related-tier inversion anchor); per the structural contrast between the paradigmatic-chaoskampf reading of the Coatepec narrative (per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml on the core-tier classification) and the inversion reading of the Bida narrative (per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml on the related-tier inversion-anchor classification); per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement that the present chapter not consult the chapter-27 Mapuche-material claims-skeleton.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The cultural-historical importance of the Bida / Wagadu narrative within the Soninke tradition and within current Mande-studies scholarship is significant: the narrative is one of the principal foundation-narratives of the Soninke as a people and one of the most-cited oral traditions of the wider Mande / Sahelian region in current Mande-studies scholarship (Conrad 2005, Belcher 1999, Hale 1998, the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition); the narrative supplies the indigenous aetiology of the Soninke diaspora and of the encroachment of the Sahara on the formerly-watered region of the historical Ghana / Wagadu polity; and the narrative's place within the wider Mande oral-epic tradition (alongside the Sunjata / Sundiata epic of the Mali polity and the wider corpus of Mande oral-epic recordings) is itself one of the load-bearing features of current Mande-studies scholarship.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions and ch. on Mande oral-epic traditions, on the Bida / Wagadu narrative as one of the principal foundation-narratives of the Soninke and one of the most-cited oral traditions of the wider Mande / Sahelian region in current Mande-studies scholarship, on the narrative's supplying the indigenous aetiology of the Soninke diaspora and of the Saharan encroachment, and on the narrative's place within the wider Mande oral-epic tradition alongside the Sunjata / Sundiata epic; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same cultural-historical importance and on the Mande-studies scholarship corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The chapter closes by registering its load-bearing methodological function for the present book as a whole: the Bida / Wagadu narrative is the present book's principal load-bearing test case for the question of whether the chaoskampf typology, when held as a Western-comparativist heuristic against a structurally-symmetric counter-example from outside the Gunkel-tradition Eurasian arc, illuminates the structural assumptions of the typology itself or whether it instead obscures the load-bearing features of the non-Eurasian narrative — a question that Part II C7 (ch. 40) is to investigate at full length and for which the present chapter supplies the principal worked instance.footnote:[Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's status as the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE FOR PART II C7 and on the chapter's setting up of the Part II C7 argument that "inversion is a comparativist artifact that can obscure more than it reveals"; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion problem comparative chapter (ch. 40 of the present volume); per the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for the present book as the principal worked instance of the structurally-symmetric-counter-example methodological move.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — direct typological contrast for the Inversion: Marduk's killing of Tiamat ESTABLISHES the cosmos and Marduk's sovereignty over the assembled gods, with the *Enūma Eliš* presenting the killing as the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty-establishing act par excellence; Mamadi Sefe Dekote's killing of Bida ENDS Wagadu's prosperity and scatters the Soninke polity, with the recorded versions presenting the killing as the destruction-of-prosperity-and-dispersal act — the same combat-and-killing structural feature with the order-establishing valence reversed at every load-bearing point. The Marduk-Bida contrast is the present book's central worked instance of the inversion at the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty level and is registered as load-bearing for Part II C7.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Anatolia and Levant (Baal-Yamm, Ch. 7) — combat-myth typological contrast: Baal's defeat of Yamm establishes Baal's sovereignty over the assembled gods and the cosmic-order distribution of authority among them, with the *Baal Cycle*'s palace-construction sequence following from the combat-victory; the Bida combat is structurally analogous in featuring a combat-and-killing of a serpentine-and-watery adversary, but with the order-establishing valence inverted (Bida is the *protector* of the city's order rather than the adversary; the slaying *destroys* the order rather than establishing it; the dispersal of the Soninke follows from the killing rather than the consolidation of the city following from it). The Baal-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 alongside the Marduk-Bida contrast as the principal worked instance of the inversion at the sovereignty-establishment level.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Hebrew Bible (Yahweh-Leviathan and Yahweh-Rahab, Ch. 8) — protective-versus-adversarial dragon contrast: the Hebrew-Bible Leviathan and Rahab corpus presents the dragon as adversary of Yahweh's cosmic order, with the slaying / binding of the dragon being the cosmic-order-establishing act in the Psalter / Isaiah / Job / Habakkuk attestations; the Bida narrative presents the serpent as *protector* of the city's order, with the killing being the order-destroying act — the inversion of the protective-or-adversarial role of the serpentine figure being the load-bearing structural feature that distinguishes the Bida case from the Hebrew-Bible material. The Hebrew-Bible-versus-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the level of the *role of the serpent* (protective rather than adversarial) within the order-establishment / order-maintenance system.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Egypt (Ra-Apep, Ch. 16) — cyclical-maintenance-register contrast: the daily Apep-overthrowing ritual of the Egyptian solar-cycle is the *cyclical-maintenance* practice in the strong sense, in which the Eurasian combat-and-overthrowing of the chaos-bringer is *re-enacted* daily as the means of maintaining the cosmic order; the annual maiden-tribute to Bida is structurally a *cyclical-maintenance* practice in the protective-serpent register, in which the human-and-divine cycle of ritual observance maintains the city's prosperity *with* the protector-serpent rather than against an adversary — and the killing of Bida *breaks* the maintenance cycle rather than re-enacts it. The Apep-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the cyclical-maintenance level (per scope.md §"Subject definition" combat-myth register 3) — the most precise structural register at which the inversion operates.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Anatolia (Illuyanka, Ch. 6) — *purulli*-festival cyclical-maintenance contrast: the Hittite Illuyanka material's *purulli*-festival ritual context is the cyclical-maintenance register in the explicit-festival-attached form, with the Tarḫunna-versus-Illuyanka combat-narrative recited as the festival's textual centre and the festival itself maintaining the cosmic-and-political order across the annual cycle; the Bida narrative's annual maiden-tribute is structurally a counterpart cyclical-maintenance practice but in the protective-serpent register rather than the adversary-serpent register, and the killing of Bida breaks the maintenance cycle rather than re-enacting it. The Illuyanka-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the cyclical-maintenance / festival-attached level.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Americas (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — order-establishment contrast at the paradigmatic non-Eurasian level: Huitzilopochtli's slaying of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua at Coatepec is read by the Mexica-area-specialist tradition (Matos Moctezuma, Carrasco, López Austin) as a paradigmatic establishment-of-cosmic-order-and-sovereignty combat — the Mexica-state-political-ritual-centre version of the chaoskampf order-establishment pattern at full strength; the Bida combat is the inversion case at the same non-Eurasian paradigmatic level — the slaying-ENDS-prosperity inversion held against the slaying-ESTABLISHES-sovereignty paradigm. The Coatepec-Bida contrast within the present book's non-Eurasian-cluster chapters is registered for Part II C7 as the principal worked instance of the *inversion within the non-Eurasian cluster* methodological move.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Pariacaca and the amaru (Ch. 26) — co-related-tier non-Eurasian chapter where the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing on grounds of an alternative load-bearing function (wak'a-succession in the Pariacaca case; covenant-and-consequence in the Bida case); the Pariacaca cycle is the *transformation* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category transforms when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing) and the Bida narrative is the *inversion* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category's structural features present with valences reversed); the two chapters between them anchor the transformation and inversion sub-problems of Part II C7 from different non-Eurasian-cluster locations within the present book — Pariacaca from the Andean cluster and Bida from the West African cluster. The Pariacaca-Bida pairing is the present book's principal worked instance of the structural distinction between transformation and inversion within Part II C7.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mapuche Kai-Kai vs. Tren-Tren (Ch. 27) — non-Eurasian-cluster co-comparandum: the Mapuche material is the South-Cone non-Eurasian comparandum to the present chapter's West-African non-Eurasian comparandum, with the two chapters between them representing the principal non-Eurasian-cluster cases beyond the Americas and Africa primary anchors of chapters 24 and 26; the Mapuche material is registered here as a forward hook for Part II C7 without consultation of the Mapuche-chapter material itself per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mwindo and Kirimu (Ch. 29) — Africa-cluster contrastive case: where Mwindo's slaying of Kirimu is followed by the hero's punishment by the sky-god Nkuba (per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml on the contrastive-case classification), the Bida narrative is followed by the abandonment of the polity rather than by the hero's punishment — the two Africa-cluster chapters between them representing the *post-killing punishment* (Mwindo) and *post-killing dispersal* (Bida) variants of the slaying-the-protector-rather-than-establishing-order narrative pattern, with the Mwindo case being the contrastive-case-tier classification and the Bida case being the related-tier inversion-anchor classification. The Mwindo-Bida pairing within the Africa cluster is registered for Part II C7 as the principal worked instance of the *Africa-cluster post-killing-consequence* methodological move.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the present chapter is a worked instance of three methodological moves: (1) the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement (scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3) applied to oral-tradition material, with the chapter opening with the Soninke tradition's covenant-and-consequence framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category; (2) the inversion-frame methodological move (the use of the comparativist inversion-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation), which the present chapter establishes for the present book and which Part II C7 (Ch. 40) develops at full length; (3) the named-but-flagged secondary-specialists pattern for non-Eurasian non-textual traditions (Frobenius, Monteil, Dieterlen, Hale, Bird, Levtzion named in body prose alongside the Conrad and Belcher anchors but not load-bearing for any individual EVIDENCE-anchored claim), per the Ch23 FC-02 and Ch26 FC-02 named-but-flagged precedent.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, Ch. 40) — PRIMARY FORWARD TARGET. The present chapter is the PRINCIPAL LOAD-BEARING TEST CASE for the *inversion* sub-problem proper (chaoskampf category's structural features present with valences reversed at every load-bearing point), to be paired in C7 with the *transformation* sub-problem cases of chapter 23 (Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi as sword-etiology / metallurgical-allegory) and chapter 26 (Pariacaca cycle as wak'a-succession). The chapter's load-bearing methodological move — using the inversion frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation — is the move that C7 is to investigate at full length, and the chapter's [INFERENCE] marker on the inversion reading and [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker on the Mande-area-specialist objection are the principal markers that Part II C7 is to engage with directly. Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE classification.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem, Ch. 41) — the present chapter contributes the *area-specialist objection to typological-assimilation* sub-problem to C8 alongside the Susanoo / Orochi material (Ch. 23), the Pariacaca cycle (Ch. 26), the Mwindo / Kirimu material (Ch. 29), the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material (Ch. 32), and the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks); the Mande-area-specialist objection is to typological-assimilation rather than to combat-myth-recognition as such, and is on the spectrum of objection-strengths between the Naumann objection in Ch23 (combat-myth framing as such) and the Salomon-Urioste-Taylor-Dean objection in Ch26 (chaos-order framing but not combat-myth framing as such); the present chapter's appropriation-protocol-compliant treatment is to use the inversion frame as the heuristic that registers the area-specialist objection rather than as a typology that imposes the Eurasian pattern on the Mande-area material.

    primary_source_opened: >
      PARTIAL. All three cited sources (Conrad 2005, Belcher 1999, Forsyth 1987) are
      print-only monographs and could not be fetched directly. Verification relied on
      trained knowledge of each volume. David C. Conrad, *Empires of Medieval West Africa:
      Ghana, Mali, and Songhay* (New York: Facts on File, 2005; ISBN 9780816052622) is
      an educational-press synthesis in the "Great Empires of the Past" series; it covers
      the Ghana/Wagadu polity, the oral tradition of Wagadu including the Bida narrative,
      and the Arabic-language chronicle parallel tradition. Stephen Belcher, *Epic
      Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1999; ISBN
      9780253212818) is the standard English-language academic synthesis of West African
      oral-epic traditions, with coverage of the Soninke/Mande Bida/Wagadu cycle, the
      Frobenius and Monteil recording strands, and the Dieterlen/École française de
      l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition. Neil Forsyth, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat
      Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746) traces the
      combat myth from Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic; it does not treat
      Mande or Soninke material. All content-claims were assessed against trained
      knowledge of the scholarly literature on Ghana/Wagadu history and Mande oral-epic
      studies.
    sources_skimmed:

        Content assessed via trained knowledge. The book covers: (1) the medieval
        western-Sahelian polity conventionally called Ghana (distinct from the modern
        nation-state), its Arabic-language chronicle designation, and the Soninke
        oral-tradition designation Wagadu; (2) the polity's conventional periodisation

        trade routes and its gold-region control; (4) the Almoravid pressure in the late
        eleventh century as conventionally linked to the polity's decline; (5) the rise of
        the Mali polity from the thirteenth century onward; (6) the narrative core of the
        Bida cycle, including the seven-headed serpent, the covenant, the annual tribute,

        Kati and successors). These contents match the chapter's factual claims anchored
