```
    triangulations_assumed: >
      (1) GHANA/WAGADU POLITY PERIODISATION: "eighth century CE through the
      eleventh-to-twelfth century CE" — per trained knowledge of the scholarly consensus
      on the Ghana Empire/Wagadu: c. 700-1100/1200 CE is the conventional range. The
      claim is accurate. PASS.
      (2) GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT: "upper Niger and upper Senegal river basins" as the
      polity's heartland region — per trained knowledge: the Ghana polity's heartland
      was in the Saharan fringe of what is now southeastern Mauritania and western Mali,
      at the upper Senegal and upper Niger river systems. CORRECT. PASS.
      (3) ALMORAVID DECLINE CONNECTION: "Almoravid pressures of the late eleventh
      century" — the Almoravid capture of Kumbi Saleh (the Ghana capital) is dated to
      c. 1076 CE in the scholarly consensus. "Late eleventh century" is accurate. PASS.
      (4) MALI POLITY RISE: "rising Mali polity of the thirteenth century" — the Mali
      Empire's rise is associated with Sundiata Keita's victory at Kirina (c. 1235 CE)
      and subsequent imperial expansion. "Thirteenth century" is correct. PASS.
      (5) TARIKH AS-SUDAN ATTRIBUTION: "seventeenth-century Tarikh as-Sudan of Abd
      al-Rahman al-Sa'di" — per trained knowledge: the Tarikh as-Sudan (Chronicle of
      the Sudan) was written by Abd al-Rahman al-Sa'di c. 1655 CE in Timbuktu.
      "Seventeenth-century" is CORRECT. PASS.
      (6) TARIKH AL-FETTASH ATTRIBUTION: "attributed to Mahmud Kati and his
      successors" — per trained knowledge: the Tarikh al-Fettash is indeed conventionally
      attributed to Mahmud Kati (c. 1468-c.1593) and extended by his grandsons and
      successors. The attribution is contested in current scholarship, and the
      chapter's "attributed to" formulation correctly registers the contested
      attribution. PASS.
      (7) SONINKE ETHNONYMS: "Soninké, Sarakolé, or Marka" — per trained knowledge:
      the Soninke are known by these multiple names in different ethnographic and
      historical-administrative traditions (Sarakolé = French colonial administrative
      term; Marka = in some Bambara/Mande usage). CORRECT. PASS.
      (8) BIDA'S SEVEN-HEADEDNESS: "seven-headed form... most widely attested across
      the recorded versions" — per trained knowledge of the Bida/Wagadu narrative as
      surveyed in Belcher 1999 and comparable sources: the seven-headed form is the
      dominant form in the recording corpus. PASS.
      (9) MAMADI SEFE DEKOTE NAME GLOSS: "Mamadi the Taciturn" or "Mamadi of Few
      Words" — per trained knowledge: "Sefe Dekote" is conventionally glossed as
      something approximating "taciturn" or "of few words" in English-language Mande-
      studies scholarship. The conventional-gloss characterisation is CORRECT. PASS.
      (10) COVENANT-AND-CONSEQUENCE FRAMING: The chapter's characterisation of the
      Soninke tradition's own framing as "covenant-and-consequence rather than
      cosmogonic-reversal" — per trained knowledge of Belcher 1999's analytical
      approach: this is the correct characterisation of the tradition-internal moral-
      didactic and foundation-narrative register. PASS.
      (11) FORSYTH DISCLAIMER WORDING: The single EVIDENCE token reads: "the
      typological framing — not Mande / Soninke material coverage, which Forsyth does
      not undertake — is what Forsyth supplies for the chapter's comparative engagement,
      and the inversion reading depends on having a typology to invert against, which
      Forsyth's long-arc anchor provides." This is the EXCEPTION case (Forsyth as

# FINDINGS

findings:

  - id: FC-01
    severity: LOW
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §Source overview, line 35 (body claim on Frobenius) and lines 59, 118 (LACUNA and
      VARIANT markers). The body claim: "Leo Frobenius's *Atlantis* series (volumes
      IV–VI), published in Jena by Diederichs from 1921 onward, in which Frobenius and
      his collaborators in the Deutsches Institut für Kulturmorphologie published their
      German-language recordings... gathered during the institute's expeditions to the
      western Sahel — with the Bida / Wagadu material drawn from named Soninke informants
      whose performances Frobenius's expedition recorded in the 1910s."
    claim_text: >
      Two sub-claims warrant scrutiny: (a) "Deutsches Institut für Kulturmorphologie"
      as the institutional name; (b) "volumes IV-VI" as the specific Atlantis volume
      range for the Bida/Wagadu material.
    problem: >
      (a) Per trained knowledge, Frobenius founded the "Institut für Kulturmorphologie"
      (without the "Deutsches" prefix) — the word "Deutsches" may not have been part
      of the official institutional name in the relevant period. The prefix appears in
      some secondary references but is not the standard scholarly citation of the
      institution (now the Frobenius-Institut at Goethe University Frankfurt).
      The institutional location was not Jena (where Diederichs the publisher was based)
      but rather Munich initially and then Frankfurt. The chapter correctly identifies
      Diederichs as the Jena publisher but the "Deutsches Institut für Kulturmorphologie"
      label conflates the publisher's city with the institute's location.
      (b) "Volumes IV-VI" is the conventional citation formula for the Sahel/West African
      material in the Atlantis series; volume 6 ("Spielmannsgeschichten der Sahel") is
      the primary locus for the Wagadu cycle. The "IV-VI" range is approximately correct
      but cannot be verified to the specific-volume level from this audit context.
      Both sub-claims are in a named-but-flagged secondary-source body claim
      (Frobenius is cited indirectly through Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005) and are LOW
      severity. However, the institutional name, if it appears in the chapter draft,
      should be corrected or verified.
    evidence: >
      The chapter's EVIDENCE token for line 35 is source_id=belcher-epic (Belcher 1999
      is cited as the mediating source for this named-but-flagged content). The Frobenius
      bibliographic details are body-prose content rather than load-bearing EVIDENCE-
      anchored claims. The institutional name and volume range are descriptive context.
    recommended_fix: >
      Before chapter-draft: (a) Verify the official institutional name for Frobenius's
      institute in the 1910s-1921 period. The standard citation is "Institut für
      Kulturmorphologie" (without "Deutsches"). If Belcher 1999 uses "Deutsches Institut
      für Kulturmorphologie," follow Belcher's usage and note it as Belcher's
      formulation; if Belcher does not use the "Deutsches" prefix, drop it. (b) Verify
      the specific Atlantis volume that contains the primary Wagadu material (most likely
      vol. 6, "Spielmannsgeschichten der Sahel," 1921). The "IV-VI" range citation is
      acceptable if Belcher 1999 or Conrad 2005 uses this formula; if they cite more
      specifically, follow their citation. LOW-priority — these are named-but-flagged
      secondary-source bibliographic details, not load-bearing factual claims.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: NOTE
    category: note
    location: >
      sources.yaml — Hale 1998, Bird, Levtzion 1973, Dieterlen, Frobenius 1921,
      Monteil 1953 are named-but-flagged secondary sources not currently in sources.yaml.
    claim_text: >
      (APPROPRIATION-FLAG body text, line 153) "Hale, Bird, and Levtzion are named in
      body prose at this marker per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern
      (Hale's *Griots and Griottes*, Indiana University Press 1998; Bird's Mande oral-epic
      publications; Levtzion's *Ancient Ghana and Mali*, Methuen 1973 — none currently in
      sources.yaml; deferred to inventory-audit per Ch23 FC-02 and Ch26 FC-02 named-but-
      flagged precedent)."
    problem: >
      Six secondary sources are named-but-flagged in body prose and not in sources.yaml:
      (1) Hale 1998 (*Griots and Griottes*, Indiana University Press);
      (2) Bird (multiple Mande oral-epic publications, no full citation given);
      (3) Levtzion 1973 (*Ancient Ghana and Mali*, Methuen);
      (4) Germaine Dieterlen (via École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest — no specific
      publication cited);
      (5) Frobenius 1921 (*Atlantis* IV-VI, Diederichs);
      (6) Monteil 1953 ("La légende du Ouagadou").
      The named-but-flagged pattern is correctly applied (per established precedent) and
      none of these sources are load-bearing for any individual EVIDENCE-anchored claim.
      However, the chapter draft will need these sources in sources.yaml before the
      chapter-draft stage, both for bibliographic integrity and for the APPROPRIATION-FLAG
      to be fully supported.
      NOTE on Bird: "Bird's Mande oral-epic publications" is cited without a specific
      title or year. Per trained knowledge: Charles S. Bird was a major Mande oral-epic
      scholar at Indiana University; his key works include "Heroic Songs of the Mande
      Hunters" (1974) and contributions to Mande oral-tradition scholarship through the
      1970s-1980s. A specific citation should be identified before chapter-draft.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml: no entry for Hale, Bird, Levtzion, Dieterlen, Frobenius, or Monteil.
      Brief special_instructions: no specific instructions on these secondary sources
      beyond area-specialist citation policy. APPROPRIATION-FLAG body text: explicitly
      acknowledges the sources.yaml absence and defers to inventory-audit.
    recommended_fix: >
      Before chapter-draft stage, add the following entries to sources.yaml:
        id: hale-griots
        source_tier: secondary
        author: "Hale, Thomas A."
        title: "Griots and Griottes: Masters of Words and Music"
        year: 1998
        full_citation: "Hale, T.A., *Griots and Griottes: Masters of Words and Music*
          (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1998)."

        id: levtzion-ancient-ghana
        source_tier: secondary
        author: "Levtzion, Nehemia"
        title: "Ancient Ghana and Mali"
        year: 1973
        full_citation: "Levtzion, N., *Ancient Ghana and Mali* (London: Methuen, 1973)."

        id: bird-mande (with specific Bird publication identified before chapter-draft)
        id: frobenius-atlantis (with confirmed volume number)
        id: monteil-legende (as Monteil 1953 IFAN publication confirmed)
      Dieterlen's specific publication anchor should be identified at chapter-draft;
      the standard anchor for Dieterlen's Mande-area ethnography is likely associated
      with the IFAN corpus. This is a pipeline-management NOTE, not a factual finding.
      The named-but-flagged pattern is correctly applied at the claims stage.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: NOTE
    category: note
    location: >
      Line 35 (body claim on Frobenius expedition timing) and lines 38-39 (body claim
      on Monteil dating).
    claim_text: >
      "performances Frobenius's expedition recorded in the 1910s" (line 35);
      "originally 1924; later expanded edition 1953" (footnote at line 38-39).
    problem: >
      (a) Frobenius expedition timing: "1910s" is approximately correct for the West
      African expeditions that produced the Atlantis Sahel material (major expeditions
      1909-1910 and 1912-1914). However, the most directly relevant expedition for the
      Wagadu material may be the 1912-1914 West Africa expedition rather than a "1910s"
      expedition in the strict sense. This is LOW-PRECISION for a named-but-flagged
      source cited indirectly.
      (b) Monteil dating "1924 / 1953": per trained knowledge, the canonical citation
      for Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" is 1953 (IFAN, Dakar). The "1924"
      component may refer to an earlier partial or limited-circulation version, but
      this is uncertain. The footnote at line 38 correctly characterises Monteil as
      "originally 1924; later expanded edition 1953" — if Belcher 1999 supports this
      dating, PASS; if Belcher cites Monteil only as 1953, the "1924" component should
      be removed or qualified. This cannot be confirmed without direct access to Belcher
      1999. LOW-PRECISION for a named-but-flagged source cited indirectly.
    evidence: >
      Both items are in footnote text for named-but-flagged secondary sources cited
      indirectly through Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005. Neither is load-bearing for
      any individual EVIDENCE-anchored factual claim.
    recommended_fix: >
      Before chapter-draft: (a) Confirm Frobenius expedition timing against Belcher
      1999's characterisation of the Atlantis recording expeditions. If Belcher says
      "1910s," retain; if Belcher is more specific (e.g., "1912-1914 expedition"),
      update. (b) Confirm the Monteil "1924" dating against Belcher 1999 or Conrad
      2005's citation of Monteil. If neither source supports "1924," drop it and cite
      Monteil as 1953 only. Both are LOW-priority named-but-flagged bibliographic
      precision checks.
```

```
chapter_number: 27
slug: "mapuche-kai-tren"
title: "Kai-Kai vs. Tren-Tren (Mapuche)"

sources:
  primary:

source_anchoring_notes: |
  Five Mapuche-specific anchors were pinned in sources.yaml at the start of this
  chapter-claims cycle (commit pending), resolving the inventory.approved.yaml
  identifier_missing flag for the mapuche-kai-tren entry. The principal narrative
  anchor is Koessler-Ilg 1962 (Cuentan los araucanos); Foerster 1993, Bacigalupo
  2007, Course 2011, and Faron 1964 are secondary area-specialist anchors used
  for the cosmographic / ritual / appropriation-framing material. Earlier
  ethnographic compilations (Latcham 1924; Lenz; Augusta-Moesbach 1934-1936)
  and colonial-period Spanish witnesses (Rosales SJ ~1670; Núñez de Pineda
  ~1673) are not yet pinned; they may be added at chapter-draft if a specific
  narrative detail genuinely requires direct citation, otherwise the
  named-but-flagged pattern (Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 precedent) applies for any
  scholar named in body prose without a sources.yaml entry.

variants:
  classification: co-equal
  summary: |
    Regional Mapuche variations across Nguluche, Huilliche, Pehuenche. The chapter presents
    the common narrative core while acknowledging regional variation, pending the
    ethnographic-source pinning at glossary-lock.

summary: |
  The marine serpent Kai-Kai raises the sea to drown the earth; the mountain serpent
  Tren-Tren raises the mountains in response to save the Mapuche. A two-serpent cosmogony
  that preserves a flood-and-height memory. The combat is between two serpent-beings rather
  than a single hero against a single monster; the pattern does not map cleanly onto
  chaoskampf.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: |
    Central in Mapuche cosmogonic tradition; oral-tradition attestation recorded in
    19th-20th century ethnography.
  function: |
    - Cosmogonic (flood-and-height origins; geographic formation)
    - Aetiological (serpent-rivalry frames the elevated Mapuche landscape)
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - Two-serpent combat rather than hero-versus-serpent — structurally atypical.
    - Flood-and-height motif echoes flood narratives across Pacific Rim traditions
      (without implying transmission).

target_length_words: 2500

special_instructions: |
  Short family-style chapter (~2,500 narrative words). The five Mapuche anchors above are
  now pinned (sources.yaml commit pending), so the chapter can proceed with proper EVIDENCE
  tokens routed through Koessler-Ilg 1962 as the principal narrative anchor and Foerster /
  Bacigalupo / Course / Faron as area-specialist secondary anchors. Area-specialist
  citation policy applies: no comparative move precedes a specialist-sourced framing of
  the Mapuche material. The two-serpent structure (Kai-Kai and Tren-Tren both serpent-
  beings) means the chapter must note this atypicality relative to the book's hero-
  versus-monster default pattern — this is why the story is RELATED rather than CORE.

  Strong [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] candidate per the Ch26 Pariacaca template: Mapuche-studies
  area specialists (Foerster, Bacigalupo, Course, Faron — and Latcham as precursor) read
  Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren as cosmogonic-cataclysm and ngillatun-ritual-foundation rather
  than as cosmos-versus-chaos combat. Register the named area specialists, the substance
  of the cosmogonic-cataclysm-rather-than-chaoskampf objection, the tier-implication
  clause, and the Ch40/Ch41 forwarding clause.

  Post-conquest filtering caveat applies (Diego de Rosales SJ ~1670; Núñez de Pineda
  y Bascuñán ~1673; later Latcham/Lenz/Augusta-Moesbach 1934-36 ethnographic compilations
  filtered through Spanish-language transcription). Register at chapter level with a
  [LACUNA] marker analogous to Ch24 / Ch26.
```

```
chapter_number: 28
slug: "bida-wagadu"
title: "Bida and the Founding of Wagadu (the Inversion)"
chapter_anchor: "ch-28-bida-wagadu"

chapter_type: "related (full, inversion anchor)"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: conrad-west-africa
      loc: "Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780816052622"
    - id: belcher-epic
      loc: "Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780253212818"
  secondary: []

variants:
  classification: co-equal
  summary: |
    Multiple oral recordings from 19th century (Monteil, Dieterlen) onward differ on Bida's
    number of heads and the hero's lineage. The chapter presents the common narrative core
    with explicit noting of regional and recorded-version variation. Tradition is medieval
    (the Wagadu / ancient Ghana polity).

summary: |
  The seven-headed serpent Bida protects the Soninke city of Wagadu in exchange for an
  annual tribute of a young woman. When the bride's suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote kills Bida
  to save his beloved, Wagadu is abandoned, the rains cease, and the people scatter.
  Slaying the serpent ENDS prosperity — the inverse of the standard chaoskampf outcome.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: |
    Central in Soninke foundation tradition; significant as type-case for West African
    oral-epic tradition (Belcher 1999).
  function: |
    - Foundation-narrative (origin of the Soninke diaspora and the end of Wagadu/ancient Ghana)
    - Aetiological (the rains' cessation and the Sahara's spread is explained)
    - Moral-didactic (the cost of breaking a covenant)
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - The "inverse chaoskampf" reading is a 20th-century comparativist frame; the Soninke
      tradition frames the story as covenant-and-consequence, not cosmogonic-reversal.
    - Similar covenant-serpent patterns exist in other West African traditions; the chapter
      flags these without assimilating them.

target_length_words: 5500

special_instructions: |
  PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE FOR PART II C7 (inversion problem). The chapter's framing is
  crucial: open with the Soninke narrative on its own terms (covenant, tribute, consequence
  of breaking the covenant) before invoking the "inversion of chaoskampf" frame. The
  inversion reading is a modern comparative overlay — flag this explicitly in the chapter,
  per inventory.approved.yaml notes. Area-specialist citation policy applies: cite Conrad
  and Belcher by name in the first factual pass; acknowledge the oral-tradition recording
  history (Monteil 1924 and later). The chapter sets up the Part II C7 argument that
  "inversion" is a comparativist artifact that can obscure more than it reveals.
```
